HIS PHYSICAL CONDITION
MAJOR EDIE DECLARED HE HAD
TOO MUCH TO DO.
JJothing to Show He Had Ever Ap
plied Money He Received for
His Own Private
Purposes.
It Is beginning to look brighter for
Lieutenant Neall. Three witnesses have
testified, and there has been nothing said
that shows anything more than the ef
fects of overwork and sickness. The
prosecution has not finished, however,
ahd there may be stronger evidence In
reserve, but so far the defense ems to
be making out a good case on the two
graver charges, conduct unbecoming an
officer and a gentleman, and conduct
prejudicial to good order and military
discipline. /
The examination of Major Edie was
concluded during the morning session
of : the court. He said he believed the
state of health in which Neall was at
the time of his disappearance was due
.to his failure to fpllow the directions
given to him to go to bed and stop work
ing. When Edie had given this advice
Neall said he had too much to do, and
he would be all right in a few days, any
way. Edie had told him it was a very
serious matter, and he would not be all
right unles he took a rest.
The questions of the judge advocate
then turned on the statement of the wit
ness made the day before, to the effect
that Neall was keeping his strength by
the use of alcoholic stimulants. He
asked the witness .what he meant by
that statement. Edie replied that
Neall's appetite was gone, his food did
him no good,_ and it was necessary that
he take some stimulant to keep up his
strength.
"Was it by your direction he took
these stimulants?" asked the judge ad
vocate.
"It was not," said the witness.
"Would the omission of any part of his
many duties have any effect upon the
abnormal state of his health?"
"lt would exaggerate lt."
"Looking back, would you modify your
opinion as to the condition of his health
ln February last?"
"I should say his ill health was great
ly aggravated." '_J
'Were his many duties beyond his phy- j
sical or mental strength?"
"Ceertainly they were. He should not '
have been doing anything."
Captain Danes then asked if these:
stimulants Neall took would not partly
account for his inability to attend to his ,
duties. "1 never saw him too much un
der the influence of liquor to attend to
his duties," answered the witness. "I
know he was not .'ting, and he had to '
keep his strength up with alcohol."
'iijat ended the examination of Major
L_ie. and he gave place to Major Field, I
Second Artillery, inspector of artillery :
and Inspector general of the Department !
of California. Major Field told how he
had Inspected the accounts of the post
exchange, but he had not been able to
make a complete inspection owing to the i
absence of Lieutenant Neall. When he \
first ordered' Neall to prepare for the in
spection he was asked to postpone It for
a time, as Neall -said he was then set
tling with the Eighth California Volun
teers. On February 15, however, be was
at the Presidio on other duty and he told ,
Neall he would inspect the exchange
that afternoon at - o'clock. Neall said
he would be ready, and Field went on
with his other duties. He went to the
exchange at half-past 2 and found Neall
was not there. He asked for an orderly
from the adjutant, and the orderly
hunted for the missing officer all over
the post, hut he could not be found.
Under cross-examination it was at
tempted to show that Neall had consid
ered that the time of 2 o'clock was con
ditional upon the duties the inspector had j
to perform, and that perhaps Neall be
lieved the inspector could not be there on {
time, but Field said he waited during
the whole afternoon and still Neall re- i
mained away. He said that he did not I
consider a wait of half an hour any
thing out of the way, but he thought for
the officer to remain away the whole af
ternoon was a circumstance that cer
tainly required explanation. He knew
Neall as a punctual and conscientious
soldier, and when he did not appear was
the first time he suspected something ,
was wrong. He was afraid then the ac
counts were not all right.
Lieutenant F. W. Harris, adjutant of
the post, was the next witness. He told j
how on the noon of the day Neall dis
appeared he received and receipted for !
his order to duty as officer of the day for
February 16, a matter that Mr. Thornton |
announced he did not contest, although j
the accused had pleaded guilty.
Continuing, Harris told now Mrs. Neall
had come to him and told him her hus
band had disappeared and she was left
destitute. He told also of the action of
the officers at the club" who raised money
for her. He said he had not received the
dividends from the exchange which
should have come to him for the use of
the regimental fund and the band, and
he had spoken to Neall about it, but had
received no satisfaction from him. He
told the adjutant he could not make any
return until he had had indorsed by Mrs.
Nolan, as administratrix of the estate
of her husband, who had been Neall's
predecessor In charge of the post ex
change, certain check made payable to i
Lieutenant Nolan. Harris referred the
matter to the commanding officer, Col
onel Compton, but here Thornton put in â 
his first objection of the trial. He ob
jected to have the witness tell what
Compton had said to Neall. as it was
iearsay, and the Judge advocate wlth
rew the question until Colonel Compton
could testify himself.
Harris then said he had received from'
Major Edie. who had got them from Mrs.
NealL a bundle of checks relating to ex- !
change business, and these he had turned
over to the exchange officer. Lieutenant
Slavens. He looked over the checks be
fore he turned them over and he saw
that some were made to the "officer of
the post exchange," some to Lieutenant
Nolan and others to Lieutenant Neall. !
He was sure none of them had been in- i
dorsed by Mrs. Nolan.
He was also questioned about a hill to i

though there was a check from Captain

date. Further cross-examination showed
that it was on January 25 that Neall
spoke to the adjutant about the checks

PEDDLING BOGUS TICKETS.
A Well-Dressed Fraud Tries to Im
pose on Two Priests.
Rev. Father Wyman of St. Mary's
Church, on Dupont and California streets.
Monday reported to the police that a
well-dressed man had called upon him
and endeavored to sell him some tickets
for a picnic which, he claimed, was to be
given by the American Association on
May 4 at Shell Mound Park. Thinking
the man was a fraud he refused to have
anything to do with him.
Subsequently the man met Rev. Father
Nugent of St. Brigid's Church and also
endeavored to victimize him. Father Nu
gent has informed the police that no
such picnic is to be held and says that
the man Is a fraud, .It is claimed that
the schemer represents himself as a Dep
uty Sheriff and that his object in selling
the tickets for the alleged picnic is to
raise sufficient money to furnish a libra
ry for the "hard-worked . boys" in the
City Hall. _â :*â ; \ -v ;-
A description of the man was sent to
the various outside stations last night
with written instructions to the patrol
men to arrest him.
SCOTT GETS THE CONTRACT.
Will Build the New Ferry Boat for
the Valley Road. ;
The directors of the Valley road have
i decided that the big ferry-boat they have
} been planning shall" be constructed by
the Union Iron Works and the contract
i will soon be let. The boat is to be used
; In the transportation of passengers and
' freight between the company's docks in
i this city and their terminus at Point
Richmond. It will be constructed on mod
ern lines In every respect save that it will
be a side-wheeler. , â¢
A contract will also soon be let to the
Fulton Iron Works for the building of a
! tug to be used by the company. Both
these matters were decided during the
, present week and the contracts will soon
; be framed.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL ; 12, 1899.
UNIVERSITY REGENTS
â¢ THANK TWO STUDENTS
SUMMER SCHOOL EXTENSION
MAY BE CURTAILED.
Two students of the University of Cali
fornia, J. W. Craig and E. H. Denicke,
were tendered the thanks of the Regents
yesterday for their work last Thursday
in preventing the destruction by fire of
the Mining 'building at Berkeley. Their
presence of mind and prompt action, it
was acknowledged, had prevented an ex
tensive conflagration.
At the same meeting the Regents ap
pointed President Kellogg emeritus pro
fessor of Latin, the appointment to date
from July 1 next.
Charles M. Bakewell, a graduate of the
university, now at Bryn Mawr, Pa., col
lege, was appointed assistant professor
of philosophy, the appointment to take
effect July 1, 1900. H. E. Montague was
appointed Instructor ln philosophy to act
during the absence of Professor Howi
son.
The principal business of the remainder
of the session was In connection with the
proposed extension of the summer school
at Berkeley. â¢ President Kellogg stated
that the proposition to enlarge the course
so as to take pedagogy, mathematics
and history, in addition to physics and
chemistry, seemed to the faculty at the
university doomed to failure during the
coming summer by reason oLthe fact that
many of the teachers who wtuld be bene
fited by. such enlargement would be at
Los Angeles during the time of the sit
ting of the National Educational Asso
ciation. For that reason he asked that
the board rescind its order for the three
additional subjects. There was consider
able discussion on the matter, which. was
finally compromised,' on motion of Regent
Rodgers, by the appointment of a com
mittee of five to arrange for the addition
al course and formulate plans for the re
ception of such of the delegates to the
association as might be induced to go to
Berkeley. The chair appointed President
Kellogg and Regents Rodgers, Kirk,
Budd and Pardee.
The thanks of the Regents were ex
tended to Mrs. Phebe Hearst for a collec
tion of books on art and architecture
Rufus N. Grant 'was appointed master
carpenter of the Wllmerding School at a
salary of $1000 a year.
The Regents authorized the expenditure
of $1675 on the Johnson building on Sut
ter street, providing Nathan, Dohrmann
& Co., the lessees of one of the stores,
would make certain alterations. This
amount makes the investment of the
university In the Johnson building an
even $400,000. Y',*"'" â 
For the Naval Academy.
Congressman Loud will have the nam
ing of a cadet to the Naval Academy at
Annapolis this" year, and there being a
large number of candidates for the honor
in his district, the Fifth, he has arranged
to have a competitive examination. He
has requested Dr. A. L. Brewer of San
Mateo; Albert I.yser, principal of the
John Swett Grammar School of this city,
and Frank J. Symmes to conduct the "ex
amination, and all three have consented.
The examination will begin- Friday at 1
p. m. In the- Lincoln School, and will in
clude the grammar school studies in ad
dition to such algebra, geometry and
general history as the. registrar of the
Naval Academy may require.

AT LAST FAILS
TO SERVE HIM
Boastful Pickpocket Is
Convicted.
VERDICT QUICKLY REACHED
SLICK THIEF BOOKED FOR THE
PENITENTIARY.
Henrioulle's Tale of How the Light-
Fingered Rascal Relieved Him
of Two Purses of Gold
Seals His Doom.
We, the jury, find the defend
ant, John Piggott, guilty of
grand larceny as charged in
the indictment. J. B. Mulcahy,
foreman; Walter A. Janke, John
Garrity, E. H. Lange, Claus
i Bardenhagen, F. N. Martinotte,
I William Goldstein, J. A. Code,
j Chris Nobman, A. D. Spearman,
| C. E. Darling, Walter Carter.
John Piggott, yho is regarded by the po
lice as one of the cleverest pickpocKets
in the country, but who has heretofore
defied the authorities to convict him, was
declared guilty of grand larceny by a jury
In Superior Judge Cook's court last night,
and will be sentenced Saturday. . The jury
was out about fifteen minutes.
Piggott was charged with picking the
pocket of Gregoire Henrioulle on a Mission
street car while returning from the Ingle
side racetrack on March 11 of this year.
Henrioulle had two purses in his pocket,
one containing $34 and the other $100 in
gold. When he started to leave the car
at the corner of Mission and Seventh
streets he felt a hand in his pocket. He
turned quickly and caught Piggott in the
act of withdrawing his hand. He grabbed
htm and accused him of robbing him.
Piggott denied the charge, but as he did
i so Henrioulle saw him passing something,
| presumably one of his purses, to Childs.
The victim of the pair promptly collared
the pickpocket's accomplice and held them
both until the arrival of an officer^
The purse containing the $34 was found
on the platform of the car at Childs'
feet. The other purse was not recovered,
but when searched at the City Prison five
$20 gold pieces were found in Plggott's
socks. The claim was made that the
clever thief had made away with the
purse and slipped the gold under his
â waistband, allowing them to drop into his
socks while being taken to prison.
The trial of the case was begun yester
day afternoon. Assistant District Attor
ney Black conducting the prosecution and
Attorney George D. Collins representing
the defendant. Henrioulle was the first
witness and told of the occurrence as re
lated above. His testimony was given in
a convincing manner, and cross-examina
tion failed to shake It.
H. H. Hutchins, who was on the car
when the trouble occurred, testified that
he saw Piggott drop something. Thomas
Connolly, the conductor of the car, Henry
Newman, a passenger, and Patrick J.
Parrell. the arresting officer, testified in
corroboration of the robbery and the sub
sequent finding of the purse at Childs'
] feet. Police Sergeant Martin and Detec
: tive Gibson testified to searching the pris
oner and finding the gold In his shoes,
and Detectives Silvey and Eagan testified
I as to his general reputation being bad. '
The defense sought to show that Pig
gott was in the habit of carrying his
; money in his shoes, and that it would
have been impossible for him to have
slipped the money into his socks while
being taken to the police station. Mrs.
Johanna Fredericks, Piggott's landlady,
. testified that she saw him place this par- ,
ticular $100 in his shoes before he left the
bouse on the noon of the day the 1
robbery occurred. There was also an en
deavor to, show that Piggott and Childs
were strangers, but this was successfully
: combatted by the testimony of Sergeant '
Martin and the detectives.
The defense contended that Piggott

Were Wedded Quietly.
The wedding of Miss Bessie Bathurst
Brooks and George Rosekrans- was sol
emnized yesterday by the Rev. S. S. Cry
or. The ceremony was delightfully sim
ple and was not a surprise to the friends
of the young couple. Immediately after
the rite the bride and groom left on their
honeymoon trip, and when they return
a reception will be held in their honor.
The bride is a daughter of Mr. and Mrs.
Harrison Brooks.

GOVERNOR GAGE'S APPOINTMENTS.
IT is safe to say that if Governor Gage manipulates
the patronage within his control upon the lines
which have been laid down for him by some of his
admirers' and supporters he will dispose of the Repub
lican party in a manner at once summary and effec
tive, it is not necessary at this time to recapitulate
the appointments foreshadowed by the Governor's
"friends." He said in an interview at Los Angeles
the other day that it would be difficult for him to get
down to the "newspaper level" upon any proposition.
But if he adopts the course which. the Burns touts
have laid out for him with respect to patronage he will
descend far below the "newspaper level," if not reach
the level of the slums.
Among the things which the Governor is alleged
to have in contemplation is the removal of Health In
spector Dockery. It i- said that this official is to be
dismissed in order that a place may be made for an
Assemblyman named Devoto, a Sixth-street saloon
keeper, who supported. Dan Bums throughout the
late Senatorial contest.
Dockery is one of the few men who during recent
years have endeavored in this city to discharge their
full duty to the public. Under his direction. the pure
food department of the Health Board has become a
really useful bureau. He has scattered the food adul
terators to the four winds, and has practically com
pelled the milkmen of this city to supply their cus
tomers with pure milk. Several attempts have been
made heretofore to remove him, but in every case
they have come to grief, solely because his record as
an official was too strong to be overlooked. If Gov
ernor Gage shall now supplant him with the pro
prietor of a Sixth-street grogshop he will certainly
take a long step toward getting below the "news
paper level."
The Republican party is not, moreover, in a con
dition to stand much of this sort of business. It al
ready has what is known among politicians as a
"black eye" in connection with the Senatorship, and
the rank and file of the party will not stand the adop
tion of any such political course as Governor Gage's'
fticnds among the Burns machine have prepared for
him.
The Governor is under obligations to the people of
San Francisco for his position. If he forces upon
them a machine Registrar, a machine water-front ad
ministration, a machine Health Board, to say noth
ing of a machine Labor Commissioner, they will cer
tainly resent his conduct at the next election. The
distinction of being a stanch friend of Dan Burns
will be nothing to the distinction which history will
heap upon him as being the wrecker of the Repub
lican party.
THE DEf-ITTi OF TABOR:
WITH the passing of ex-Senator Tabor o^Colo
rado the West has lost one of its most unique
figures, the Centennial State its greatest bene
factor.
The story of Tabor's sudden accession to wealth
reads like a story from the Arabian Nights. A poor
storekeeper in the almost deserted town of Gro, he
"grubstaked" a poorer cobbler, ignorant of mining.
The luck of fools and tenderfcet is proverbial, and
August Rische proved no exception to the rule. He
struck the Little Pittsburg and made himself and
Tabor. Right there the strength of character of the
storekeeper showed. While Rische began at once
to see how much fun he could get for his money,
Tabor pressed his luck. He went in for "grub
stakes" and more mines and had more luck.
Everything he touched became money. The "old
man," as he was called, was immediately surrounded
by sycophants, who, every other means failing to turn
his head, tried to make him believe that a great po
litical future was in store for him. For a time they
made him believe it, but that time was short, being
only the thirty days he served as an appointed
Senator, which short term became famous only
through the story of a $250 nightshirt that never was.
Later he was gratified with election to the position of
Lieutenant Governor, but his failure in that position
was so conspicuous, even to himself, that Tabor was
quite cured of political ambition.
While he may have been foolish politically, it. is
agreed on all sides that he was not ungrateful to the
State of Colorado for the riches he had taken from her
hills. As best he could and as quickly he sought to
repay. An opera-house in Denver which to this
day is a model for its kind, was one of his first works.
Numerous other buildings in the city, the pioneers in
modern architecture in the West, were erected at his
will. He was the head and front, with purse and in
fluence, in every, public movement as long as his
money lasted. He was a poor manager, and the end
came five years ago. Then he was forgotten until re
cently President McKinley, finding him in poverty,
making a last struggle to regain the place he had
lost, appointed him postmaster at Denver.
How has Colorado remembered such a benefactor?
Has she named a town after him or a public park or
a schoolhouse, or even one little street? . Not that the
records show. The Centennial State had almost for
gotten Tabor until his death. Maybe she will place a
ton of marble on his chest and yet call him blessed.
THE SflMOflN SITUATION.
SAMOA has caused trouble and discussion in the
world out of proportion to its size and impor
tance. The natives are among the finest of the
Polynesian people, ranking with the Maories and
Kanakas in physical presence and power and in in
tellect,. The natural tropical productions of the
islands supplied their wants without labor, and instead
of the diseases of civilization keeping their population
within the bounds "of this natural food supply, the peo
ple accomplished the same end by restricting their
meat diet to the consumption of enemies killed or
captured in war.
Christian civilization found them out at last, and
by prompt introduction of the loathsome diseases,
which are among its gifts to aborigines everywhere,
put an end to cannibalism not only by reducing the
population through sickness, but also by making the
survivors non-edible.
After this part of the. white man's burden had been
assumed and the Samoan soul had been put in the way
of salvation, there arose a desire to occupy, possess
and own the Samoan's lands. During Grant's ad
ministration an ambitious American special Consul
taught our people pet names for the islands and was
sure the republic could not survive unless they were
annexed. However, manifest destiny was not as al
luring then as now, and the natives were left alone
tc the exterminating influences of the pestilences we
had bestowed upon them.
During Mr. Cleveland's first administration the
Samoan consulship was given to a San Franciscan,
who found it slow business watching copra dry in the
sun and brought on a fight between Mataafa and
Malietoa, rivals for the kingship. His description
of leading his king out and taking him by his shell
collar and gee string, while the German Consul did
the same by the king favored of Germany, and knock
ing the two royal persons together to get them to
fight, is one of the most amusing things in our con
sular history.
This Consul thereafter ran up the flag over the
royal palace in Apia and proclaimed an American
protectorate. But, as he neglected to "nail the flag"
to the island, it was hauled down and he was called
home.
During the Harrison administration this country
invited England and Germany to assist it in the super
national task of bestowing good government upon
Samoa, the difficulties being such that only the three
strongest nations in the world could meet them.
Ever since then Samoa has had probably the worst
government ever known. The rights of the natives
have been trampled under foot.- The foreign resi
dents have jangled and backbitten each other, and
the old cannibal days have seemed like a dream of
peace compared to the turmoil and tension resulting
fiom three little men governing an afflicted people for
three big nations.
Out of this partnership of folly may issue trouble
with Germany. It is claimed that England and the
United States have violated the treaty of Berlin, in
which the partnership of the three nations was ar
ranged. It is rather remarkable that England en
couraged Admiral Kautz to bombard the bamboo
shacks of the natives and disregard the protests of
the representative of Germany. But, though we may
have been pulling international chestnuts out of the
fire for Great Britain, we must go ahead and fight
our German partner if we have to. It is part of the
white man's burden. The entire Samoan group and
all that grows there is not worth enough to pay de
cent interest on the damage to American commerce
that would follow a declaration of war against Ger
many and would fall before a blow has been struck.
Yet we are now face to face with the possibility of
such a war, and it may be noticed that England is in
a position to stay at peace and let us fight it out, with
no more serious contribution to our welfare than her
highly valued and veteran "moral support."
THE TAXPAYERS' BURDEN.
MR. JOHN J. VALENTINE, the tax
shirker of the .We,lls-Fargo Express Com
pany, having arranged for shifting the white
man's burden of taxation from himself and his com
pany to the shoulders of the general public, has
turned his pious thoughts to the brown men of the
Orient and finds cause to mourn.
In an article about as long-winded as a hypocrite's
prayer he set forth his views of the conditions of the
Malay laborers in the tropics and succeeded in get
ting it passed through the press of an evening paper
by means unknown to the public. Possibly it was by
some trick of legerdemain, a mutual interchange of
pass and repass between the parties to the play but
that'is a minor matter. The main thing is: Mr. Val
entine desires the people should know that he is
weeping over the brown man's burden.
It appears from his article that Mr. Valentine is
sadly grieved because the "cowed and patient Malays,
working under the harsh supervision of agents, pro
duce wealth, that is rightfully theirs because earned by
them on lands which have been wrested and tricked
from them," only to see that wealth carried off by
foreigners. Such grief for such a cause is undoubt
edly philanthropical, but what does the weeping Mr.
Valentine think of Americans who produce wealth
only to see it tricked from them by the tax-shirking
corporation of which he is the most conspicuous or
nament?
The burden of taxation imposed upon the people
of the United States for the purpose of raising a war
revenue would not be onerous to any if all bore their
due shar,e of it, but when rich corporations shift their
share upon others the burden becomes heavy upon
those on whom it falls and is the more irritating be
cause the unwilling bearer knows it has been imposed
upon him by the tricks and frauds of unpatriotic
shirkers of public duty.
Again and again the courts have declared that the
tax imposed upon the business of express companies
by the war revenue act must be paid by the compa
nies, that it is the duty of their agents to affix the
revenue stamps the law requires. These declarations
have been made in many States and by many authori
ties. Still Mr. Valentine refuses to obey and fights
for time. Every day of delay gives him that much
longer in which to levy tribute upon the public, and
he avails himself of it to the utmost.
Clearly the white man', burden of taxation frets
Mr. Valentine only when he cannot shift it upon
some one else. When he can accomplish the shirking
he resumes his show of piety, prepares circulars full*
of scriptural injunctions for his employes at one
time and at another prepares articles on the wrong of
robbing the brown man and passes them along.
The brown man of the Orient need not be grateful
to his unexpected champion. Mr. Valentine mourns
for the lot of the Malay only because he has no share
in the spoils. If the fortunes of war and imperialism
bring the suffering laborers of the Orient under the
domination of the Wells-Fargo Company Mr. Valen
tine would skin them alive.
If Italy insists upon having a port in China it would
pay the Empress to call in old Menelek of Abyssinia
to act as arbitrator.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APEIL 12, 1899
PURCHASING SENATORSHIPS.
IT seems that so long as the purchase of United
States Senatorships was confined to respectable
politicians, distinguished soldiers and statesmen
and public spirited millionaires, the practice of putting
them up and knocking them down to the highest bid
der did not arouse much resentment among the peo
ple. But for some time a low, disreputable class of
boodlers have been emboldened by the example of
their betters to make bids. The deadlocks throughout
the country and the adjournments of Legislatures
without election are, it is affirmed by intelligent
authority, the result of attempts to secure the toga by
men entirely unworthy to aspire to it.
The cases of Addicks in Delaware, Quay in Penn
sylvania and Burns in California point the moral
which adorns this tale. Addicks is a Republican mil
lionaire politician, and for six years he has been try
ing to "bribe the Delaware Legislature to give him the
Senatorship. A few days before the late General As
sembly adjourned three Democrats, one Senator and
two Assemblymen, voted for Addicks. There is so
little question that they were paid to do so that all
the people of Delaware have assumed such to be the
fact. The president pro tern, of the Senate struck the
name of the Senator off all the committees to which
he had been appointed, and the Assemblymen were
ostracized by their colleagues, irrespective of party.
Everywhere the three men go they are pointed out as
boodlers and denounced as having sold themselves to
Addicks. There was some difficulty, in fact, at one
time in protecting them from the wrath of a mob
which had assembled about the Capitol to lynch
them.
In Pennsylvania Quay has held the Senatorship
for two terms, with the effect of arousing an opposi
tion in his own party which bids fair to defeat him.
Quay's methods are the methods of a practical, un
scrupulous politician. He is the type of men who
are everywhere dragging the Government down to
the level of the slums, and to whose efforts to achieve
power the historians of the American republic will
attribute the results which the historians of Rome at
tributed to the Praetorian guard.
Nothing need be said of the Burns campaign in
California. All its details are still familiar to our peo
ple. The important question is, however, how are the
aspirations of the corrupt politicians who are now
aspiring to the Federal Senate throughout the coun
try to be permanently thwarted? If this class of men
arc permitted to continue their purchases of Senator
ships, in the course of time the great historic upper
house of Congress will become nothing more or less
than a refuge for vulgar millionaires and political
boodlers. The degradation which presages' the de
cline of its influence has already begun to appear in
the contempt which all classes of people feel for some
of its members.
0N INTERESTING EXPERIMENT.
NO legislative act of the past winter has attracted
more general attention than that of the Legis
lature of Michigan authorizing the city of De
troit to acquire the ownership of street railroads
within municipal limits and operate them as public
works. The experiment is certainly to be watched
with more than ordinary interest by the people of
every city large enough to have a considerable street
railway system. . . . â¢
While the municipal operation of street railways is
common enough in Europe, it has never been at
tempted on any notable scale in this country. What
is to be undertaken at Detroit is therefore a radically
new departure in American municipal administration
and will afford something like an effective test of the
policy of governmental control of public utilities of
which we have heard so much in the way of theoreti
cal discussion in recent years.
The Michigan law provides that the negotiations
for the purchase of existing lines and the arrangement
of details for operating them shall be intrusted to a
commission of three persons, to be designated by the
City Council, and that body has chosen Governor
I'ingree and two associates friendly to the plan to
carry out the work. The commission has large powers,
lCis required to report to the Council the price it is
proposed to pay for the street railways, to make an
nual reports of receipts and expenditures, to hold
books and accounts open to inspection by local
legislative authority, and is forbidden to incur any
debts whatever in operating the roads, but otherwise
its action is untrammeled.
Virtually the commission is an independent body
charged with' the sole function of operating street
railways in the interests of the community. It must
file the annual reports stated, and its accounts must
be public, but it can act in freedom and of its own
initiative, being subject in no way to the Council or
the Mayor. It is not responsible to any one even
for the amount it pays for the roads, the Legislature
having authorized it to issue bonds of the city for the
amount required â a sum estimated variously from
$12,000,000 to $17,000,000.
The undertaking is in every respect a Pingree
measure. It remains to be seen with what success he
will be able to carry it- out. The sentiment in favor
of municipal control of all local public utilities is al
ready, strong in the country, and if the Detroit ex
periment prove successful, corporations monopolizing
such utilities in other cities will have to be more
careful of the rights of the public than they have been,
as otherwise they may be forced to give up the busi
ness.

THE GOVERNOR DENOUNCED
The Paso Robles Recprd.
The Governor's failure to sign the bill to reimburse Claus Spreckels
" for the amount of money he so readily advanced to assist in relief work
last fall has caused a shudder of disgust to his (former) constituents and
others alike. It is only necessary to broach the subject to call forth a
bitter denunciation of such a policy. When the thought of asking for
relief came to the. people the thought was where and how to get it.
The Legislature was not. in session and Governor Budd was power
less to render public aid, but he immediately set to work and found a
beneficent man, Claus Spreckels, who said he would advance the money
to aid the sufferers.
' "The glad. news soon spread and "God bless Claus Spreckels" went
up from the sufferers. Imagine their Indignation when informed that
the man occupying the exalted position as Governor of the State should
not only refuse to sign the bill appropriating the money advanced, but
should reply in a taunting manner to a communication sent him (this
Governor) regarding his disposition of the bill when the time limit for
making the bill a law had almost expired.
What manner of man is this "mysterious" Governor Gage to so treat
the people's benefactor? It is little wonder that expressions of bitter
indignation are frequent when the thought that the provider of those
precious bales of hay "has been thus treated by one occupying the ex
alted position as Governor of the State of California. Did many of these
people shout for Gage and vote for him during the campaign of a few
months ago? Yes, but not to be thus disappointed without the free ex
pression of their thoughts concerning such action. The people believed
they had elevated a man to the exalted position to which he aspired
who was too broad-minded to drag a charitable act into politics. But
lo! they are doomed to disappointment.
The bill has been lost by the pocket veto system. Claus Spreckels
will not miss the eleven or twelve thousand dollars, but the Governor
has shown his hand to the people of California!
NO CHANGE HAS
TAKEN PLACE IN
THE SEASONS
An Average Rainfall
Is Maintained.
THE PROBLEM OF IRRIGATION
SUBJECT IS DISCUSSED BEFORE
BOARD OF TRADE.
Pumping Plant System Finds Favor
. With Authorities Over Present
7" ithod of Taking Water
From Streams.
The climate of California has undergone
no change,' and is undergoing none. The
average rainfall, taking years in groups
and not singly, has shown no diminution
ln the past fifty years. This was the dic
tum laid down at a meeting of the State
Board of Trade yesterday by men who
have made a part of their life work the
study of conditions which have a bearing
on the farming industry of the State.
Their deduction is that the' potency of the
forces that are at work to produce rain
are the same now as in the past, and that
they will continue in effectiveness.
The discussion as to existing conditions
in California was opened by W. H. Mills.
From a recently compiled chart he showed
the rainfall for half a century, declaring
that it was not quantity, but distribution,
which had the most beneficent effect upon
field culture. It was best to have general
rain. It should, properly, be light in Octo
ber and heavy in November. There should
be none in December or January, and then
light rains Into May. According to sta
tistics, January 10 marks tin- middle of tho
season, the rainfall up to that time being
usually one-half that for the winter. Mr.
Mills dilated upon the benefits to be de
rived from irrigation as an adjunct to the
rainfall. On this he said:
Fruit raising, vine growing and small
farming in California will depend for
their success in a very great measure
J upon the artificial application of water.
These applications have proven prof
itable and will continue to be so under
favorable conditions without irriga
tion; but they are far more profitable
with it. Every orchardist and vlne
yardist ought also to be a gardener,
and, in order to diversify the products
of the land, irrigation must be resorted
to. Clover, berries, gardens, orchards .
and vineyards should be found on. the
same holdings and should occupy the
attention of the cultivator of the soil
in their various seasons. This diversity
would in a measure equalize the de
mand for labor throughout the year
and greatly improve the labor condi
tions. Irrigation will enable the or
chardlst and vlneyardist to supply his
table with poultry, eggs, milk, butter,'
vegetables and fruits, and thus confer
upon his holding its first and para
mount duty of affording him a com
plete subsistence. The problem of liv
ing having been solved,* the question
of profit would become more certain
and, happily, less important. The an
cient methods of irrigation will be su
perseded by modern and more econom
ic methods. This revolution will be
referable to the cheapening of mechan
ical power and the increased efficiency
of pumping machinery. Under the old
method a main canal carrying water
at an elevation to lay a certain district
under Irrigation was necessary. Its
construction and maintenance were
costly, while the application of water
to lateral ditches was also costly and
unsatisfactory. A gravity supply of
water can be passed over a surface
where the decline is constant. There
are no lands sufficiently level to make
more than 60 per cent of their surfaces
subject to irrigation from any point of
elevation in their vicinity.
Naphtha or gasoline engines have
furnished a very cheap power for
pumping, "-While improvements in
pumps have made their use far more
economic than the application of
water by more primitive and crude
method of ditches. Of course, the
water must be brought to a point from
which it may be pumped economically,
and this can be done In three ways
first, by a main canal, which Is tapped
only with the suction of pumps. Such
a canal would cost less and would be
more permanent in its construction,
because it would not be constructed
with reference to supplying water to
laterals. Second, by the use of the
channels of living streams. For the
most part the irrigable lands of Cali
fornia are accessible to streams. Third,
by the sinking of wells. Concerning
the latter, lt should be noted that an
Irrigated country soon fills with water,
and wells In such a country afford an
ample supply for such portions of land
holdings as need irrigation. The eco
nomic use of the pump is also greatly
reinforced by the facility with which
In modern times electrical power Is
transmitted at cheap rates. Some
practical experiments In pumping were
made in various parts of the State
during the drought season of 189 S. It
is stated, on seemingly reliable au
thority, that there are not far from
2000 irrigation pumps in operation in the
county of Santa Clara alone. For the
most part these are using wells, and
their use has proven beneficial and
economical.
Regarding the use, of irrigation sup
plemental to the rainfall In Santa Clara
! County, the following was volunteered*
; "Many of the orchards of twenty acre
have their own pumping plants. Some ten
acre orchards have a plant and sell water
also to neighbors. Most of the larger
plants are so arranged that they also
sell water to adjoining orchards, and by
this system, which Is now pretty' well
developed, owing to the drought of last
year, the principal orchard district of
Santa Clara County is pretty well pro
tected in the way of Irrigation."
I. H. Thomas of Vlsalia, Tulare County
> contributed to the discussion the follow
ing statement as to the advantages de
rived from irrigation by use of a pumping
plant By at em. He said:
There are many advantages to be
gained by the pumping plant system
over the system of taking water
from the streams, one of which is box
ing the water under your control at
the time you need it; another is that
the water is free of the various seeds
that spread over your land from chan
nel irrigation, thereby making the
expense less in keeping the land in
good condition for the growing crops.
me of the greatest advantages with
the orchardlst is a preventative in the
shape of a severe frost. The object
lessons of the past winter in this
seclton are indisputable. Where late
irrigation and winter irrigation was
followed with good cultivation the cit
rus and deciduous orchards stood the
temperature without damage, jg while
adjoining lands, which were dry and
uncultivated, were badly Injured at a
temperature of 22 to 28. With pumping
plant ready and the temperature at
the freezing point, by filling the
ditches through the night from wells
with water at 65 degrees, it would
raise the temperature several degrees
In the orchard and in many instances
save a fruit crop that would other
wise have been destroyed by the frost.
The difference between the tempera-'
ture of the water and the frost at the
killing point, 28 degrees, is about 37
degrees. Thus it will be seen that
the atmospheric temperature would be
raised sufficiently by the temperature
of the water to save the fruit.
In his advocacy of the pumping plant
system Mr. Mills expressed the belief that
San Joaquin Valley would not be hab
itable if Irrigated by the old system
throughout its length and breadth. He
said extensive Irrigation by ditches means
a swamp district in which is found ther
mal fever and malaria.
Irrigation, he said, fertilizes the soil
and at the same time equalizes the ter
n! perature. It gives to the land plant food
when water is pumped out of a river
where it has kccumulated. In this way
the land never deteriorates, but is en
riched
Colonel John P. Irish told of the im
portance which drainage had attained at
Fresno. From long continued irrigation
the land is full of water and is commen
cing to fill the low places. This water, he
thought, should be drained off, to be used
again. He called upon Arthur Briggs of
Fresno for his views. ,
Mr. Briggs first controverted the state
ment made by -Mr. Mills that a series of
irrigation ditches would produce disease.
He said irrigation and drainage
hand in hand. On the east side of Fresno
County the old irrigation ditches of ten
years * ago are being used to draw off
the water. He believed small pumping
plants could be introduced which would
pump water from the- ditches cheaper
and tetter than by running It off through
laterals. Water should not be used more
than once, as it develops alkali in too
large quantities. To prevent this it
should be carried away through the earth
to be saturated again with fertilizing ma
terial. il is people irrigate one year in
advance to aid nature and to provide
against a possible drought. Early irri
gation with cold water checks growth, as
it chills plants and trees.
Mrs. Elizabeth Shields of Sacramento
told briefly of the system of irrigation
on Feather River and said that the pump
ing system would be tried.
Among the members present were: Gen
eral N. P. Chipman, George C. Perkins, J.
S. Em*ry. Craigie Sharp, Mrs. Elizabeth

Colonel John P. Irish, W. Frank Pierce,
Mark L. McDonald, George T. Trow
bridge, R. W. Maslin, I. H. Thomas and
C. F. Montgomery.

CALIFORNIA
OIL TO ENTER
ASIATIC MARTS
Pittsburg Firm to En
ter the Local Field.
BIG REFINERY IN FRESNO
EMERY'S AGENTS EN ROUTE TO
THE SOUTHERN WELLS.
With a Big Refining Station on Thif
Coast the State Could Con
trol the Trans-Pacific
Market.
L. E. Mallory and W. M. Graham,
members of the Emery Refining Com
-1 pany, one of the largest oil producing
â¢ firms of Pennsylvania, are registered at
the Palace from Pittsburg.
They have not come out to California
. on a pleasure trip, but as a matter of
j good solid business, which, if it is con
: summated, opens up a brilliant future
for this State's budding industry..
The primary attraction that has
brought them here is the oil wells in the
Fresno and Santa Barbara regions. The
development work done in those sections
the last year has opened up hundreds
of good producing wells, but it has not
opened up a profitable market for the
crude product. Nearly all of it must be
shipped East for refining, and the long
haul plays havoc with the margin. It
| is the intention of the Emerya to estab
i lish one refinery, and possibly more, in
| the southern fields for the purpose of
j refining the oil here and extracting its
! by-products to meet the rapidly grow
: ing demand. California ofls refined here
I ln the State would in a short time con
trol the Asiatic and Hawaiian markets,
as there would be no heavy transconti
nental frieght chargeable against them,
and they could be sold at a less price
j than the products of Eastern refineries.
If the Emery people carry out their
plan it is their intention to "ay particu
lar attention to the extraction of gaso
line.
"There is a marvelous demand grow
ing in the Hawaiian Islands," said Mr.
Mallory yesterday, "for American oils
and particularly for gasoline. There are
big pumping stations on nearly all of
the sugar plantations and gasoline en
gines are generally used. '. . . ' :
"The Anglicizing of the Orient is al
ready having its effect on our business.
The demand for coal oils, so called, and
all of the by-products of petroleum, is
increasing rapidly. To make the largest
margin of profit from trans-Pacific trade
we must establish ' refineries in this
State, which nature has generously en
dowed with a very high grade of oil.
There Is no reason why California should
not control the Asiatic market as well as
that of Hawaii. I do not care to go into
our move fully until we have made a
j tour of the southern fields and see what
are the conditions that we must meet."

Purser Bucklin Wages War on the

Unaccompanied by Slaugh
ter but by Noise.
The transport Morgan City arrived'
from Manila yesterday after a good pas
sage of twenty-eight days. Strange to
say. tho Irat assistant engineer was in
disgrace, thus making the third consec
utive sport to come in here with
trouble in the engine room.
First Assistant Mears of the Scandia
was under arrest when hat vessel
reach..d port, First Assistant Dyer of
the Arizona was left behind at Manila to
serve a -lays' sent. for mutiny, and
now First Assistant Green of the Morgan
City is In disgrace
The relation: between Chief Engineei
Kelly and First Assistant Green were
strained before the Morgan City left
Manila. When that vessel ached Na
gasaki there was an open rupture, with
the result that Green was relieved from
duty and ordered to his room. Then the
firemen came into the squabble. They
aserted that the food supplied to them
was not good, *and that Green -ot into
trouble with the chief because* he stood
up for their rights. They therefore sided
with the first assistant and fused duty.
Then there was music in the air. Cap
tain Dillon explained to the men that it
was mutiny on the high seas, and told
them plainly what they might expect on
arriving at San Francisco. hen better
counsels prevailed and the firemen went
back to work.
The trouble wns not over, however, .
and one of the firemen is now nursing
v broken head as the result of an en
counter with Captain Dillon. Gus Moore
was one of the ringleaders of the strike,
and on a number of occasions the cap
tain had to admonish him. A couple of
days ago Moure was very insolent and
Captain Dillon ordered him below. The
tinman got furious and jumping for
ward struck the captain on the point
of the Jaw, felling him to the deck. Dil
lon was senseless for a few moments,
but when he got on bis feet again he
was not slow to act. Seizing an old
Mauser rifie, thai was lying on the
deck, by the barrel he used the butt as
v club and knocked Moore down. The
latter's scalp was cut open and he was
badly used up. â¢id ay when the
Morgan City docked his head was cov
ered with bandages and he was sick and
sore all over.
( if course there is going to be an in
vestigation. Green has complaints to
file not only against the chief engineer
but also against the captain. He ac
cuses the latter of conduct unbecoming
an oifieer of a transport and a gentle
man, but Captain Dillon laughs at the
charges and shows a letter from First
Lieutenant Thomas M. Moody, Twen
tieth Infantry, in charge of the men,
who went to Manila on thi Morgan City,
which thanks Captain Dillon for his
kindness and courtesy to everybody
aboard the transport during the voyage.
There ems to have been a series of
misunderstandings on and about the
Morgan City, lt was generally under
stood that she was to bring the remains
of 450 soldiers here for interment. In
stead of that she only brought the bod
ies of Lieutenants D. D. Alford, James
Mitchell and Ed A. Hailing. In army
circles the remains of the heroic sol
diers were also expected, and when the
Morgan City* passed Alcatraz the fort
began firing the minute guns. Captain
Dillon says it was generally supposed
at Manila that the bodies would be sent
up on the transport, but the order was
countermanded.
Seven discharged soldiers came up on
the Morgan City in charge of Captain
Christy of the Twentieth Kansas. They t
are: Edward Franklin of the Hospital
Corps, Sergeant E. O. Augestine, Private 1
John Short. Private John Clements and :
Sergeant Charles Peare of the Eighteenth i
Infantry, Private John Loftus of the
Twenty-third Infantry and E. A, Ander- !
son of the First North Dakota*.
The Morgan City will be overhauled and ;
rcprovisioned and got ready for a return i
trip to Manila as quickly as possible. She
is sure to be ready by the 2"th inst., but
If lt can be managed she will sail this
day week.
The Canadian Pacific steamship Tartar
arrived yesterday from Vancouver. The
Tartar is from Hongkong and first went
to British Columbia to discharge her
cargo intended for transit hv the Cana
dian Pacific Railway. The Tartar made
the voyage from Hongkong to Vancouver
in thirty-six days, from Kobe in twenty
seven days and from Yokohama In
twenty-five days, while she ran from Co
mox* to this port in seventy-six hours,
She had one orst-class passenger and
sixty-seven Japanese. The Tartar will

RETURN OF THE MORGAN CITY.
IT was generally expected that the transport would bring back from Manila the remains of 450 Soldiers
who fell in the Philippines. As the vessel passed up the bay the battery at Alcatraz began firing min
ute guns" and kept it up until the Morgan City was anchored. The transport only brought up the remains
of three lieutenants. Eight discharged soldiers also came up on the vessel.
fonts net, and was built in ISS3 by Aitken
& Mansel in Glasgow. She is 367 feet 5
Inches long, 47 feet 2 inches broad and 30
feet 3 inches deep. She is owned by the
Canadian Pacific Steamship Company,
and purchased by them last year from
the Albion. Steamship Company of South
ampton, England. This Is her first visit
to San Francisco and nearly her entire
inward cargo is for Chinese merchants.
The inability of the steamers coming to
this port to handle all the freight offer
ing in China necessitated the putting on
of the Tartar.
The British ship Dunstaffnage arrived
yesterday from Calcutta in 116 days. She
is laden with Jute and jute bags. The
Dunstaffnage passed Pltcarln Island on
March 5, undjr a fresh southerly wind,
which took her along at a clipping gait.
On January 2?. in latitude 44 degrees south
longitude 1"" degrees east, while in very
heavy weather, an ordinary seaman. God
frey Mackeller, was washed overboard
and drowned. The vessel was hove to, a
boat was lowered and a careful search
was made for the lad's body, but with
out success. The boy was but 19 years
of age.
.The bark John D. Tallant made the
trip from Hakadate to this port in the
remarkable time of twenty-nine days,
having arrived here early yesterday
morning. The captain is quite proud of
his voyage, as it has not been equaled
for many years. The Tallant brought
14.485 mats of sulphur. WftMj
Three vessels containing sugar arrived
from Honolulu yesterday. They are the
barkentine Newsboy, nineteen days; the
schooner Allan A. eighteen days, and the
barkentine W. H. Dimond, sixteen and a
half days.
The British ship Harland. on which 7-l_
per cent reinsurance has been paid, was
spoken yesterday in latitude 1 north lon
gitude 25 west. She was bound from this
port to Liverpool.
Captain John Barnesson has been ap
pointed assistant quartermaster by the
United States Government, and will take
entire charge of the transports from the*
loth inst. Captain Barnesson once com
manded the Arizona. He has been on
shore duty for quite a while attending to
the loading and getting away of the
transports. Some two months ago Cap
tain Barnesson marie a flying trip to
Washington, and It was then understood
along the front that he would on his re
turn succeed Captain Howard, who has
been anxious for some time past to re

THEY WERE WED IN
A BOWER OF ROSES
George E* Bennett and Miss Susie F*
Hall Made One in St Stephen's
Church*
AVERT pretty wedding was celebrated in St. Stephen's. Church last
evening. The contracting parties were George E. Bennett of the
firm of C. W. Haseltine & Co. and Miss Susie F. Hall, and the cere
mony was performed by the Rev. E. J. Lion.
A bevy of young ladies under the direction of Mrs. J. M. Hall had
been engaged all day in decorating the Interior of the church, and
the sacred edifice was a veritable bower when the bridal party entered the
building.
Miss Hall was attended by Miss Edith H. Booker as maid of honor and
Miss Jeanette Clabrough and Miss J. May Windham as bridesmaids and Ed
na Bennett and Doris Hutchins as flower girls. The groom was attended by
Dave Ewart. The ushers were: Fred Heywood, Dr. J. Chalfant,. L. H.
Smith and Robert T. Legge. The church was crowded and a shower of con
gratulations poured in- on the happy couple after the ceremony.
Mr. Bennett is well known in San Francisco business circles and has a
host of friends. He was secretary of the Winter Cotillon Club, it was
at one of its dances he met hie wife. Mrs. Bennett was a society belle and
has almost as many friends as her husband. They will spend their honey
moon In the southern part of the State.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 12, 899.
will be docked at Folsom-street wharf.
The Scandia has been on the drydock
and is now at Pacific-street wharf. The
matter of loading the two transports is
being hurried to enable them to get away
for ' Manila at the appointed time.
The new turntable of the Market-street
Railway system will shortly be in opera
tion, and while the finishing touches are
being put on no teams will be allowed to
cross East street for the next ten days.
They will be either sent down Mission
or Steuart street.
The fight between the sailors and the
ship owners promises speedy development
within the next few days. Sailors are
pretty scarce on the front now. owing to
the large departures for the north of
fishing vessels, and those remaining are
determined that the proposed increase
which has been advertised shall he paid.
The captains of the vessels in port are by
no means pleased at the prospect of pav
ing $30 a month, and it is understood that
this rate will be fought. If such is the
case there will be some interesting de
velopments.
The Christian Endeavorers appeared
before the Harbor Commissioners yester
day and asked for berth privileges so that
they can tie up their launch in which
they visit ships. The Endeavorers ex
plained that they cannot afford to pay
a man to look after the boat. The matter
was referred to the Chief Wharfinger.
The Christian Endeavorers will get a
berth. i
Took the Case Under Advisement.
The case of John Plunk-it, charged with
battery by Mrs. Rebecca Samuels, was
heard by Judge Morgan yesterday. The
allegations made by Mrs. Samuels, as
heretofore published, were controverted
by witnesses for defendant. As there was
a conflict of evidence Judge Mogan took
the matter under advisement. He gave
notice that he would render his decision
on Friday morning.
Kline Is Exonerated.
The charge of felony embezzlement
preferred against Christian Kline, the
San Rafael butcher, by the Marin
County Packers' Association, was dis
missed by Judge Mogan yesterday. The
Judge found that Kline had sunk a for
tune- in the concern, and had purchased
the sheep, the money for which he was
accused of embezzling, with his own
money.

WEAVING THE NET
AROUND FOSTER
DISPUTE AS TO THE OWNERSHIP
OF TWO RACE HORSES.
Several Witnesses for the Prosecution
Testify as to Admissions Made
by the Defendant.
The preliminary examination of Fred
Foster, the horseman, charged with ob
taining money by false pretenses by A.
Howard, the Australian plunger, was con
tinued before Judge Conlan yesterday.
C. P. Waterhouse, of the firm of Burns
& Waterhouse, testified to Foster having
purchased the horse Yellow Tail from his
firm for $10,000 and that Howard's name
cut no figure in the transaction. He knew
Foster only.
Charles A. Thorpe, Sl9 Guerrero street,
testified that he had no recollection of
having a conversation with Foster about
the purchase of Yellow Tail. The prose
cution places considerable importance up
on this statement of Thorpe, which will
develop before the case is through.
Edward K. Moffitt, 309 Howard street,
testified that Foster told him he felt the
losses on Howard's horse Yellow Tail
more keenly than Howard because he
had stood sponsor for the horse when
Howard purchased it. Alexander Wilson.
520 Bush street, testified also that .Foster
admitted to him that Howard was out
JSOOO or 39000 on the transaction.
. Cassius Sloan, the jockey, testified that
Foster held out $500 on him which should
have been paid from Howard to him. On
another occasion Poster had assumed ig
norance as to who Howard was.
George Adams, the representative of a
London grain-buying house, testified as to
having seen a written paper in whioh
Foster admitted over his own signature
that the horse belonged to Howard, and
on another occasion Foster had said that
he did not own one hair in the horses'
tails. < ; i .-.â â *-.;.
The examination will be continued this
morning.

As Seen in the Biograph.
The initial public exhibition of the won
derful series of moving pictures of Pope
Leo XII I will be given at Metropolitan
Temple on Monday, April 17. The pleas
ure of a pilgrimage to Rome, with the
added grace of the Papal benediction,
i will be accorded those present upon the
momentous occasion. The pictures (com
prising 17,000 negatives) were taken re
cently at the "Vatican gardens by the
American Biograph Company. Among
the eminent clergy who. have had an op
portunity to see the pictures are the
j Apostolic Delegate Archbishop Martlnelli,
Cardinal Gibbons, Archbishop Corrigan,
i Mgr. Conaty of the Catholic University,
I Bishops Donohue and Curtis, Dr. Whit
> ney, president of the Georgetown Uni-
I versity. Â»
Among other interesting and historic
scenes to be depicted by the biograph
upon this occasion will be a number Â;
views of the coronation ceremonies of the
Queen of Holland.
THE FIGHT FOR DECENCY.
John Carroll, Proprietor or Phono
. graph Parlors, on Trial.
John Carroll, proprietor of a phono
graph parlor at 724 Market street, ap
peared before acting Police Judge Barry
yesterday morning to answer a charge of
having indecent pictures in his possession.
Policemen Tyrrell and Esola, who made
the arrest, testified to having viewed the
pictures in Carroll'- parlors, and produced
a number which they had seized, which
the Judge examined.
* Carroll had no defense to offer except
that he would leave it to the Judge to
say whether the pictures could honestly
be called indecent. The Judge said he
would give his decision to-morrow.
Wolf's Bondsmen Sued.
Suits were filed yesterday in- the Su
perior Court by the heirs of the late
Sarah Maria Pearson against the sure
ties on the bond of John Wolf, executor
of decedent's estate, who disappeared
mysteriously from this city some time ago
with the funds of the estate. The sureties
on the bond, which was one- of $44,000,
are H. W. Wreden, William G. Wreden,
William Blester, Edward Lundstadt, John
W. Morsehead, David B. Faktor, G. H.
Walker and August Petzold. Sarah Ma
ria Robson and Mrs. Allison, daughters
of the deceased, instigated the present
suit and ask that they be given judgment
against the sureties for the sum of
$1120 40 each, such being, it is set forth,
their share of Wolf's shortage, which
amounts to $9237 32.
OUR SOLDIERS
in the PHILIPPINES
YOU are proud of the fine fighting spirit shown by
our Regulars and Volunteers in and around Manila
in the last month. It is something to hear and read
of it; it is far more to see, by means of photographs, the
charging troops, the men on firing line, the batteries in
action, the trenches, and the battle-fields with the Amer-
ican and Filipino dead x as they fell. HARPER'S
WEEKLY, dated April 8, has 28 pages, 4 of which con-
tain photographs (23 pictures) of these scenes. Buy it
on the news-stand before you go home
HARPER'S WEEKLY
MANY BUYERS
OF REAL ESTATE
IN THE MARKET
Week's Deals Aggre
gate Half Million.
MONEY SEEKING INVESTMENT
BUSINESS MEN AND CAPITAL
ISTS CONFIDENT.
Big Blocks Change Hands in the
Wholesale District Involving
â¢ Large Sums, and Others
Being Negotiated.
San Francisco capitalists and business
men are showing their confidence in the
future of th. city in a manner that ad
mits of no criticism and which is ample
evidence that this city is entering upon
an era of prosperity greater than it has
experienced for years. They are putting
their surplus capital in real estate and
they are putting it out in large sums, as
the records afford undeniable proof. The
real estate transactions for the month of
March foot up to a total of over. $1,500,000,
and the record for the present month
promises to be double that -.mount. In
the first week in April transfers of real
estate amounted to $587,500, and a num
ber of deals that are now under way
with every prospect of being consum
mated within the next ten days will swell
this sum to nearly a million. 4V
Following are some of the larger deals
that have been closed since the month
opened:
North corner of Mission and Main streets,
$116,000; Nos. 3 and 5 Front street â¦.6,500; north
line of O'Farrell street, 197:6 went ot Powell,
$86,000; east line of Sansome street, 89 feet
south of California, $65,000; east line of San
some street, 118 (eel south of California, $58,000;
northwest corner of Van Ness avenue and Va
llejo street, $32,500; northwest corner of Eddy
and Larkin streets, $24,000; northwest corner of
Polk street and Fern avenue, m.500; east line
of Spear street, betweeri Howard and Folsom.
$20,000; west line of Ste\iart street, 275 feet
south of Market.'- $20,000; southeast corner of
O'Farrell and Powell streets, $60,000.
In addition to the above many transac
tions involving smaller amounts were re
corded of property transfers In the
Western Addition. Pacific Heights and
other outlying districts. A. M. Speck &
Co.t Bovee, Toy & Sonntag, Thomas Ma
gee & Son, Baldwin & Howell, Madison
& Burke, G. H. Umbsen & Co. and Easton
& Eldridge are the principal firms
through which the deals were engineered.
Money Is cheap and plentiful in San
Francisco just at this time and investors
are seeking for good property, large
blocks having the preference. There is a
well defined movement toward that sec
tion south of Market and east of First,
and the nearer the water front the prop
erty is located the easier It is to dispose
of it advantageously. Business men real
ize that .money invested in real estate
that can lie utilized for warehouses and
so located as to do away to a certain
extent with drayage charges will bring
in larger returns than almost any other
vestment. The prospect of a third
transcontinental competing railroad is a
strong factor in this confident feeling,
and while there is a general upward
movement in real estate of all kinds the
big deals for some time to come will be
confined to what is included in the whole
sale district. B-Bj-BltMil.'l'-IB
Young Men's Catholic Union.
The members of Leo Assembly No. 4,

Tenth and Market streets, last Thursday

versary. After short addresses, compli

High School Debate.
An interesting debate between the senior
and junior classes of the Humboldt Even
ing High School took place last evening.
The subject under discussion was: "Re
solved, that alliance should be established
between the United States and Great Brit
ain for their mutual protection and inter
ests." Bert Schlesinger, who acted as
judge, rendered a decision in favor of the
supporters of the negative side of the
question. The junior class was represent
ed by Herbert Arthur .and Thomas F. Duf
fy and the senior class was represented
by W. G. Mellish and J. C. Corbett.
Bonds Not Sufficient.
Bernard Ward, ex-financial secretary of '
the Marine Firemen's Union, who was
held to answer on two charges of felony
embezzlement by Judge Conlan on $9000
bonds, was ordered Into custody by the
Judge yesterday, as he did not consider
the bonds furnished by Ward sufficient.
Ward surrendered himself at the City
Prison. His attorney will apply for a writ
of habeas corpus to-day asking for his re
lease on the ground that Judge Conlan
had no right to order him into custody, as
he lost jurisdiction In the cases when
Ward was held to answer.
In the Divorce Court.

Margaret K. Smith from James Smith
on the ground of failure to provide; Julia
A. Schoen from A. V. Schoen on the
grounds of failure to privide, intemper
ance and Schoen's conviction of a felany;
and Eva R. Viera from Joaquin Viera on
the ground of willful neglect. Charles
11. "Walker has sued Annie Walker for
a divorce, alleging desertion as. a cause
of action. . - t

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE MARKETS.
Sugar marked up.
Silver unchanged.
Wheat lower and quiet.
Barley futures declined.
Oats, Corn and Rye unchanged.
Less demoralization ln Hay.
Bran doing better. Middlings unchanged.
Beans steady and In fair demand.
Potatoes and Onions still depressed.
Vegetables about the same.
Butter and Cheeese unchanged.
Eggs beginning to weaken.
Poultry still firm. Game weak,
Cherries and Strawberries sell well.
Oranges. Lemons and Limes unchanged.
Nothing new in dried Fruit.
Provisions steady to firm.
Quotations for spring Wool fixed.
Hides quiet and Tallow firm.
Hops quiet and unchanged.
Meat market steady.
Oils unchanged.
NEW YORK DRIED FRUIT MARKET.
The New York Commercial says:
"Recent information confirms what has j
been previously stated in this column re- ;
garding the rasin situation on the coast, j
and also points out that supplies of graded .
loose muscatels are greatly reduced, and
are held much suffer in consequence. Hold
ings la this market are relatively small,
and are held very firm at about previous
prices. The principal change is noted ln the |
Indisposition to shade prices to secure business, i
The association is not likely to make another
advance on this crop, but individual holders j
may conclude to mark prices up a fraction j
as spot stocks clean up.
"â¢Currants are firmer on recent advices from :
Greece, which report the first hands market ,
as much stronger on reports of crop damage,
which are not understood here. Some 5000
tons are now on passage from Greece to Eng
land and Germany, reducing available stock- i
in Greece to 18,000 tons. Spot demand is
larger, and prices are a trifle firmer. Hold
ers refuse to sell, present prices net them so
much loss, and all are taking as much ad
vantage as possible of the change in the Greek
market. , â¢ -: â .'"-:_'â¢_
"Prunes are unchanged. There is said to
be an attempt in progress to secure control of
all available supplies here and on the coast.
It is uncertain how large the purchases
would have to be to do that, but it appears
from what can be learned tliat "It wouldn't j
require a large sum of money to do it.
The difficulty purchasers meet is the firmer j
feeling which prevails and the indisposition j
of holders to let their stocks go. even when
full prices are offered for the entire supply,
Prices are reported as likely to advance
shortly on all larger and some medium sizes.
The conditions governing th" market are i
especially favorable to holders, and Indicate
a satisfactory situation during the remainder
of the season." "

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
The weather is cloudy and threatening over
Washington, Oregon, the Rocky Mountain re
gion and Southern Arizona. Light rain has
fallen in Washington and Oregon during the
day, and high southerly winds have prevailed ]
along the coast.
The pressure has fallen west of the Rocky
Mountains.
The temperature has remained about station
ary in all districts.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, April 12.
Northern California Fair Wednesday; light
northwest wind.

west wind.

light northwest wind.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, April ILâ Bullish confidence
prevailed in Influencing stock prices to-day,
which advanced almost continuously, slight set
backs regularly bringing new buyers. The
final dealings were In large volume, bringing
the total to nearly three-quarters of a million
chares. There was continuous support by large
operators, accompanied by optimistic utter
ances. Potent allies of higher prices were the
showing of the Government crop report in win
ter wheat, which was better than expected,
spring weather in the West, abundance of
money at easier rates and Washington advices
that a sudden call on the depository banks for
the funds to pay the Spanish indemnity was ]
not contemplated, but that the withdrawal of
Government funds would be gradual as hith
erto. A sentimental influence was the knowl- !
edge that a Presidential proclamation that we â¢
are at peace with Spain would be issued to- j
day. The grangers were liberally purchased
for Western account. The Vanderbilt Issues
continued ln active request. Local transporta
tion lines continued Btrong, Brooklyn Rapid '
Transit making an extreme gain of almost 9
points. The other Flower specialties were like
wise heavily purchased, People's Gae moving
up over 4 points. Continental Tobacco's rise 'â 
of 6% was in part associated with to-day's
meeting relative to a new stock issue. The
Southwestern group was active and strong, as
were the iron and steel shares. In fact before
the day was ended practically everything was
higher, the larger gains being among the spe
cialties. Commission house purchases increased
with the strength of the market. London was
but a small factor, selling about half as much
as It purchased. Call money began with large
loans at 6 per cent and gradually easing off
to iiÂ®3 per cent. The ease of money was re
flected In an appearance of more stcength in
the sterling exchange market, part of which
was possibly due to anticipatory purchases in
connection with the payment of the Spanish
Indemnity. Several foreign exchange authori
ties claimed to have noted no such buying
hitherto, which lead, to the belief that the
settlement was yet to be arranged. The mar
ket close, strong at about the best prices.
The bond market followed stocks but to only
a narrow extent. Total sales, $3,035,000.
United States old 4a and coupons advanced '
% in the bid price.
The total sales of stocks to-day were 72L905

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK. April 11.â Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
The markets here were idle to-day on talk of
dearer money. The close was firmer. Ameri
cans opened above parity and were steady till
afternoon, when they became strong on New
York buying. The features were New York
Central, Atchison preferred. Union Pacific and
Reading firsts. The contango was 4%Â®4%, on
St. Paul It was %, Anacondas 8%, Bostons 2%.
New York is still buying Juniors of Mexican
National, and London houses are puzzled
thereat.

vious Saturday:

Cornâ United States and Canada, east of the
Rockies, Increase 351,000 bushels.
Among the more Important Increases reported
to Bradstreet's, not given In the official visible
supply statement, are those of 342,000 bushels at

bushels at Galveston and 141,000 bushels at
New Orleans.

bushels at Northwestern Interior elevators and
106,000 bushels at Milwaukee private elevators
Tlie aggregate stocks of wheat held at Port
land, Or., and Seattle and Tacoma, Wash de
creased 89,000 bushels last week.
CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, April, 11.â The Impulse among
wheat speculators at the start was to sell
on the Government crop report, which was
regarded as bearish. May opened %c lower
at 71% c down to 70% c, and as there was no
great amount of wheat wanted to begin with
the price declined steadily .to 70% c. The de
cline was accentuated by a report from Kansas
City to the effect that the Kansas crop had
without doubt Improved wonderfully |in the
last few days. The more the Government re
port was considered 'and analyzed the more the
trade was Inclined to doubt if it could right
fully be given a bearish interpretation, and
many of the early sellers wanted back former
holdings. May wheat rose from around 70% c
to 71% c. In the end the market began to yield
to the little peeping note of Improving pros
pects in Kansas and to turn a deaf ear to the
perfect howl of distress from* other quarters.
Liquidation set ln on a heavy scale and the
market lost the advance about as fast as It
gained it. May closed with sellers at 71V4^j
71% c, a net gain of %c.
The corn market kept In touch with wheat.
There waa a fair cash demand. May closed
unchanged. .
I o*ts were fairly active,' with trade of a gen-
I eral character. May closed %c higher.
I Liquidation weakened provisions. Cash trade
1 showed some little Improvement, but the stocks
1 of lard are accumulating fast and bulls in this
I product were discouraged because the May and
j June run of hoes is now here. Pork lost 15c,
' lard 7%c and ribs To.

DECIDUOUS AND CITRUS FRUITS.
Fourteen boxes of Cherries came In and sold
at $1 EoÂ®2 per box.
Arrivals of Strawberries were 24 chests and
brought good prices.
There is no change in citrus fruits, the mar
ket being freely stocked with all kinds.
This bids fair to be a flne fruit year. Stocks
of canned and dried fruit have run down to
almost nothing, and owing to the shortage In
the Eastern crop the canners and driers are
making preparations to handle more than the
I usual quantity. The crop bids fair to be spot
ted, as the latest advices report more or less
of a drop almost everywhere. There will prob
ably be plenty of fruit, though the crop will
I hardly be enormous, as many have thought
up to the present.

HIDES. TALLOW, WOOL AND HOPS.
Quotations for new Spring clip of Wool are
now given. The market remains very dull.
There is nothing new in Hops.
Hides are quiet and prices are considered too
high in comparison with Leather. The circu
lar of W. B. Sumner & Co. says:
"The hide business remains about the same
as last quoted. Prices continue at liberal fig
ures and sales are fairly active of certain
grades of hides and there id little or no sur
plus stock ln the market. Harness and Skirt
ing Leather has been quite active during the
past season and the better grade of hides com
mand liberal values. We are Inclined to think
that the present figures on hides will continue
for some time at least. For the local market
we quote: Wet salted heavy steers are In de
mand for harness and skirting; wet salted me
dium and light steers are fairly active; wet
salted cows sell readily; wet salted veal, kip
and calf are active and rule at full rates.
"Owing to the dry seasons which have pre
vailed in California during the past year the
tallow output has fallen off ana the stocks
on hand at the present time are very small.
Tallow has advanced about 1 cent per pound
since March 1, and the supplies at present
received are hardly sufficient for the de
mands of trade. We look for an active tal
low market."
