THE STOCK MARKET.
There was a fair business In local securities
yesterday morning. The, Increased gas rates
gave Gas and Electric more tone, and there was
some recovery in prices, though not so much
as might have been expected. Sales were made
at $75@75 50. Hawaiian Commercial was some
what higher at $91 50. Other stocks remained
about the same.
On the afternoon call business was quiet. Gas
and Electric stock weakened off to $74 87%.
Mining stocks were stronger, though business
continued quiet. The telegram from the pump
said: "Elevator working steadily, keeping the
water below 1850-foot level. The water in the
winze from the 1750-foot level Is IS feet 5 Inches
below the station.'"
At the bond session of the San Francisco
Stock Exchange 25 Hawaiian Commercial sold
at $91. 10 Sun Francisco Gas and Electric at
$74 50 and $1000 of United States 3 per cent bonds
at JICS.
Of the British Columbia mines the Rambler-
Cariboo paid a dividend of $10,000 on the Ist
Another, the War Eagle, will pay a dividend
of $26,250 en the 15th. The Payne has declared
Its regular dividend of $25,000 for the
month, payable on the 10th of April. The aver
age shipments from the Payne are about 250
tons of ore per week, which net $50,000 every
month, half of which is paid in dividends. Be
sides these dividends bonuses are given during
several months of the year. During 1898 there
were bonuses for September, October, Novem
ber and December.
There will be no dividend paid by the Na
tomA. Vineyard Company this month.
Tie Challenge Consolidated reports $1949 cash
on hand April 1.
The Consolidated Imperial had $1357 cash on
hand.
The following ore shipments were made by
rail from the mines .of Eureka district and
vicinity in Nevada for the week ending April
7: Bull whacker mine, 37,480 pounds; Diamond
42,830; Eureka Consolidated, 78.280; Leonie,
10,720; Oriental and Belmont, 7240; Richmond,
146,640; Williamsburg, 4160.
The report of General Manager Ross of the
Comstock Pumping" Association for the past
week reads as follows: "The pumping asso
ciation has been engaged in cleaning out and
repairing the C. and C. shaft below the 1760
--foot level. The shaft is in fair condition. The
pump compartment is nearly full of air pipes
columns, straps, pump rods and bobs, that are
difficult to take out. This compartment must
be cleaned out. as the space Is required for the
second elevator that will be placed ln this com
partment before the water can be lowered by
the first arranged elevator. The plan is to
place the second elevator in the pump compart
ment at as great a depth as possible; then to
start and hold the water at a point that will
allow us to break the Joints In the elevator that
Is now at work and raise it to the surface of
the water, when such changes as are required
can be made In the throat and nozzle, then to
lower it Into the water again and submerge it
as deep as possible.
"When the water has been lowered the pump
compartment will be cleaned out to as great a
depth as possible and the elevator In this com
partment will be again lowered to the point
where the required changes can be made ln the
first elevator. In this way of working the 600
feet of flooded levels will be drained. Judg
ing from our experience in draining the shaft
for 160 feet below the Sutro drain boxes, it has
been found that if an elevator was designed
for the full lift of 600 fee/ and it could be sub
merged below that point it would drain the
C. and C. shaft for that distance In from three
to four weeks: but It would not be safe to at
tempt such work. The water Is being drained
from the lower levels slowly, while being rap
idly lowered In the C. and C. shaft, as our
measurements taken at the Consolidated Cali
fornia and Virglna winze, the Hale & Norcross
incline and the Chollar combination shaft in
dicate.
"It Is quite possible that nearly all of the
drainage from the various mines finds its way
through the vein and not through the drifts
as our experience with Comstock drifts and
levels Indicates very clearly that there are but
few places where these openings will not cave
tight enough to form fairly strong dams.
"Since April 1 the water has gone down In
the Chollar combination shaft 5 feet 1 Inch. In
the Hale & Norcross incline 12 feet 6 inches and
in the 1750 winze 16 feet 9 inches, while being
lowered 120 feet In the C. and C. shaft. No
measurements were possible in the Osblston
shaft until April 6, when we found the water
to be 52 feet 11 inches below the station of the
drift connecting with the Sutro tunnel. The
water in the C. and C. shaft Is being kept down
to about its lowest mark, 132 feet below the
1760 level, by running the elevator* sixteen hours
during each twenty-four. We expect to con
tinue working in this way until the second ele
vator is in place. The pumping association has
been engaged as usual in doing work for the
several mining companies, as appears in their
weekly reports."

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located In the Merchants* Exchange. Is
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to visit- the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters.
of Interest to ocean commerce. ,
The time hall on the tower of the new Ferry
building at the foot of Market street is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Naval
Observatory. Mare. Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any. is published
ln the morning papers the following day.
W. Si HUGHES.
Lieutenant. U. S. N.. In charge.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day ln the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right . hand column gives the
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions- to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (-) precedes the JjeighL
and then the number given is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower. low waters.

SEN. PERKINS
MAKES SOME
COMPARISONS
McKinley in Contrast With Gage
and Dan Burns as a Moral
Senator.
A Good Word for Hanna and HoarâDig
nified Method of Disciplining In
flated Senators.
OAKLAND, April 11.â For one hour
to-night Senator G. C. Perkins divulged
secrets, dropped significant remarks and
launched criticisms teeming with sugges
tiveness before a large and Influential au
dience in the First Congregational
Church. The address was perfect, not so
much for what the Senator said as for
what he inferred and said by indirection.
Senator Perkins took occasion to let his
audience know his opinion of Governor
Gage, Dan Burns, Senator Hanna and the
late Legislature and several other per
sons and things.
"Of course," said California's sole Sena
tor, "these are not criticismsâ
comparisons made in a social way. The
chairman has assured me ere are no
scribes here, so of course I feel free."
And then the speaker looked toward the
men with pencils and paper and pro
ceeded.
"I am not going to criticize," said Sen
ator Perkins, "but as I stand here I can
not help contrasting the methods of our
truly great President with that of the
Governor of California, of this great
State. Do you know that out of all the
bills introduced into the Senate President
McKinley vetoed just one and pocketed
another? What did we see at Sacra
mento recently? Hundreds of bills that
were passed by he Legislature are
tucked away in what must be one of the
most monstrous pockets m the State.
Surely the last Legislature must have
been %â¢ ry indecent or else* . Presi
dent McKinley is neither arrogant nor
has;;.. lie is truly treat. He says to
himself tnat if Congress in its wisdom
has been informed on measures, has de
bated them and has passed upon them it
Is but justâ not to .-ay courteousâ to be
lieve that its members are conscientious
and intelligent; it is but right that I
should sign them.
"When the President saw in the appro
priation bill a large item for educational
purposes he at once put his pen to it.
When our Governor saw a bill appro
priating a few thousand for our State
university he thought our great institu
tion should not have too much support
and the bill disappeared, In a similar
instance Governorâ now Presidentâ Me-
Kinley of Ohio showed such force and
sagacity that all America wanted him to
tase its scepter. 1 do not wish to criti
cize anybody here to-night, but a contrast
between two men may be interesting."
Senator Perkins did not propound the
query, "What would Dan Burns think of
the United States Senate and what would
the Senate think of Dan Burns?" but
the majority of his audience asked them
selves the question and heard the speaker
answer it.
"The religious complexion of the Senate
Is â I," said : Senator' Perkins- - "Nearly
all the members have some distinct reli
gious connection. I have never, in six
years, seen a United States Senator go
into a saloon. Neither have I seen one
ever do anything that they need be
ashamed of. They are, without exception,
men of sterling character, unimpeachable
morals and examples of statesmanship.
All attend church and all are sincerely
desirous of doing what is right and what
their constituents want.
"One of the most Impressive moments
in the Senate is the opening of a session.
Rev. Dr. Milburn has not seen the light
of day for thirty-five years and for half
of that time he has been chaplain of both
houses of Congress. As he is led into
the Senate by the Vice President every
Senator stands and as those sightless
yes are directed to heaven every one
feels an inspiration from his words. I
have heard him repeat that sentence in
the Lord's prayer, 'Lead us not into
temptation ,' so that it seemed like a
voice from God. And, believe me. it met
with a response from every one of those
ninety Senators, as they joined in the
solemn 'Amen.'
"I cannot forget that the California
Legislature at its last session did not de
cide upon a man to go to the United
States Senate, but if the choice had to be
made by the people at the ballot box
there would have been a Senator elected,
and 1 am In favor of amending the consti
tution so that this can be done."
Senator Hanna of Ohio, according to
Senator Perkins, has been grossly mis
represented by a' portion of the press.
After referring to the modern methods of
journalistic criticism, the speaker said:
"Senator Hanna is a much better man
than he is credited with being. Since his
boyhood he has been the architect of his
own fortune. He is a man of high char
acter, good standing in society and good
habits. He is a hard worker and Senator
Hanna has never to my knowledge cast a
vote in the Senate that will not stand the
Roentgen ray of investigation, no matter
from where directed. Don't credit all that
a partisan press says about United States
Senators, for all are good men, although
perhaps no more infallible than the Pope
of Rome.
"But though ail are good men, some are
th* reverse of practical. They do not
catch the spirit of things easily, and have
to be disciplined. There is a very digni
fied system in vogue In the Senate to
teach men their measure. It is by get
ting up and leaving a member talking to
empty seats and the galleries. One of
these is Tillman of South Carolina, who
Is said to have been thrown into the Sen
ate with a pitchfork. He is a good man
at heart, but cannot appreciate the fact
that the report of a committee is hardly
ever altered by argument on the floor.
However, Tillman talks and talks and the
gallery listens, and when the bell rings
for a vote the Senators file into their
seats.
"One day I asked who was speaking,
and was told it was Allen of Nebraska,
who was bellowing at some cattle on the
plains. On another occasion some one
asked who was talking, and the reply was
'Morgan of Alabama, who Is always talk
ing but never says anything.' Morgan is
a pleasant speaker, but he never changes
the sentiment of the Senate. He talks
too much.
"Senator Proctor was the man I once
asked if it was possible .to live economi
cally in Washington. I meant could a
man live on his salary of $5000. He re
plied "yes," that he was not economical,
but his wife was. and they lived in a
house for which they paid $8000 per year
re v nt ' That's economy in Washington,
where there are two classes of societyâ
Â°ne ytth appetites without dinner and
the others with dinners without appetites
âand that explains all.
"Senator Hoar has been abused by Call

Senator Perkins referred personally to
over a score of his confreres in the United
States Senate, and said that the office of
Senator means from six to sixteen hours
of work every day. Referring to the
breadth of the American character, he
said that in the Senate at its last session
I "there were twenty men" who had once
tried to destroy this GovernmentâConfed
j crate soldiers, and fifteen men who had

He closed with a forceful tribute to
President McKinley, and said: "When the
next two years of this administration
have closed I am confident that all will
acknowledge the Christianity, wisdom,
diplomacy and patriotism of our Presi
dent, who has this day proclaimed that
we are as a nation once again at peace
with Spain."
The address was given before the Young
Men's Congregational League.
! LITTLE ROSE YON SCHMIDT
NOT FORCED TO TESTIFY
Coroner's Jury Returns a Verdict of
Murder and Suicide in the Awful
Dual Tragedy.
ALAMEDA, April 11.â the'
jury, do find that the name of the de- I
ceased was Isabelle yon Schmidt, a
native of Australia, aged 32 years,
and that she came to her death at
2232 San Jose avenue at about
12:30 p. m.. April 9. 1899, and that
death was caused by a knife wound in j
the heart inflicted by Edward A. yon '
Schmidt with murderous intent. We,
the jury, do find that the name of j
the deceased was Edward A. yon j
Schmidt, a native of California, aged i
about 43 years, and that he came to j
his death at 2232 San Jose avenue at !
about 12:30 p. m., April 9, 1899, and !
that death was caused by a knife
wound in the heart inflicted with
suicidal intent.
Such were the verdicts rendered by the
Coroner's jury impaneled to investigate
the cause of death of Mr. and Mrs. E. A.
yon Schmidt, the principals In Sunday's
horrible tragedy. Before Coroner Mehr
mann commenced the Inquest he informed
the jury that although a murder had
been committed the murderer had placed
himself beyond the law by taking his own
life, consequently no testimony would be
introduced tending to show the motive
of the crime or the condition of the mur
derer's mind at the time of the killing.
No facts not already fully exploited in
the public prints were brought out.
Charles L. Tilden identified the remains
of Mrs. yon Schmidt and Deputy Coroner
Fowler gave similar testimony relative
to the remains of the dead woman's ex
husband. Mary Thompson, the nursegirl,
recited the story of the tragedy, and after
some other unimportant testimony the
jury returned the verdicts as given above.
The jury was composed of R. B. Decker,
W. G. Vollmar, A. Innes, C. W. Damon.
George X. Gray, R. H. Fallmar. R. M.
Mitchell and William W. Graham.
Little Rose yon Schmidt was not present
at the inquest. Coroner Mehrmann con
cluded that it would be cruel and in
human to force the little one to recite the
horrible details of Sunday's tragedy. The
funeral of Mrs. yon Schmidt will take
place to-morrow morning from the under
taking parlors of C. H. Wever, 1504 Park
street. All during the day the compan
ions and playmates of the Yon Schmidt
children have been carrying flowers to
decorate the casket containing the re
mains of the unfortunate mother.
FUN FOR COLORED TROOPS.
Visiting Soldiers to Be Entertained
by Local Admirers.
When the colored troops arrived from
the East recently and went Into quarters
at the Presidio their brothers and sisters
of color of this city and Oakland deter
mined to make their stay here as pleasant
an possible. To this end a special com
mittee of arrangements, consisting of
Major L. A. Penny and R. P. Keeling,
started the ball in motion. These gentle
men, with others, worked hard during the
late war with Spain to raise a regiment
to go to Cuba. to fight the Spanish, and if
hostilities had been prolonged they would
no doubt have been sent to the front.
Hence the selection in arranging for the
entertainment of the soldiers of â¢ the
Twenty-fourth United States Infantry.
The first in this line will be a banquet to
the visitors to be held this evening in
California Hall. 620 Bush street", under the
auspices of the Army and Navy Union.
A large number of invitations have been
sent. out to citizens and the affair prom
ises to be a success in every way..
The next feature in the way of enter
tainment will eclipse the banquet, because
of the greater possibilities. A moonlight
picnic, social and general good time will
be held in Germanla Gardens at Harbor
View on the evening of the 19th Inst. The
band of the Twenty-fourth Infantry will
be in attendance and any amount nf fun
may be expected. A general invitation
has, been extended to the residents across
the bay and this city. Major Penny and
Mr. Keeling are the leaders in the ar
rangements of this affair, assisted by an
honorary committee consisting of Ser
geants Plrla of Company E, Brown of
Company H, Powell of Company I, Thom
as of Company H, Johnson and Washing
ton; " yy*
STOLE A JAR OF GUM.
William Pepper, Â£.ged 13, was arrested
last' night by Officer C. Green and
charged at the' City Prison with petty
larceny. The prisoner and a companion
named Carl Sevens entered B. Brown's
candy-store, at 1656 Market street and
boldly grabbed a Jar of gum and rushed
away with it The proprietor pursued th.
young thieves, and his shouts attracted
Green's attention and he captured Pep
per, Sevens getting away. ' .
For the past two weeks complaints
have been filed at police headquarters by
candy dealers who have been similarly
robbed by youngsters, who peddle their
spoils on the street. It is believed Sevens
will be arrested to-day.
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 12, 1899.
RIOT AVERTED
BY OFFICERS
WITH RIFLES
Quarrymen Object to
Bill of Fare.
STRUCK FOR MORE PAY
1
WOULD STOP OTHERS FROM
WORKING.
Chief Fletcher and a Few Men With
Winchesters Soon Clear
. the Field of the
l Strikers. .
Oakland. Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, April 11.
The timely appearance of a large divis
ion of police armed with short, wicked
looking Winchester rifles probably pre
vented bloodshed at the Piedmont quarry
this morning. The trouble was caused by
a disagreement over the bill of fare put
up by the boarding-house keepers at the
quarry. ."â¢;'â¢'
Last Saturday about a dozen men quit
work and their places were filled on Mon
day morning with new men. The strikers
did not move away, but hung around,
threatening trouble. Last night the po
lice were asked for an officer, and Detec
tive Hodgkins was sent out. All was quiet
till this morning, when the strikers gath
ered around and caused it to be known
that they would interfere with their suc
cessors, and as some of them were
armed, a riot was imminent. )
Chief Fletcher, Captain Wilson. Detec
tive Shorey and Officers Curtis. Andrews
and Pardee filled the patrol wagon and
with their guns went to the quarry. Chief
Fletcher ordered the strikers to go to the
office, get their time, and then vacate the
premises. There was very little opposi
tion, the sight of the officers, and espe
cially the Winchesters, having a pro
nounced effect. Two police officers, armed,
were left on the scene and will not be
withdrawn for a day or two.
"The principal grievance of the strik
er.,'' said one of the company's officials,
"Is that they are not treated properly by
the boarding-house keepers at the quarry.
They claimed that the food furnished was
not good enough and the sleeping quarters
not to their liking. Finally, they demand
ed that their wages be Increased from
$1 50 to $1 75 a day. The company was
unable to satisfy the demand or the men
for better food and quarters, as the board
ing-house keepers leased the place, and
the men need not stay there unless they
desired to. They could board anywhere.
The wages paid by us are good and we
could not accede to a raise."
TURNED SUDDENLY INSANE.
Mrs. Anna Dockhan of Oakland
Creates Considerable Excite
ment at the Ferry.
Mrs. Anna Dock nan. the divorced wife
of the editor of the Haywards Review,
created considerable excitement at the
ferry yesterday morning. She came
across from Oakland with her daughter,
a bright girl 9 years of age, to purchase
tickets for Belding. Mich., where her
brother, -William Coulter, lives, but ap
peared to suddenly become Insane. Ser
geant : Avon endeavored to pacify her,
and was struck in the face, receiving a
black eye. Her screams attracted a
crowd, who watched the struggle be
tween her and the sergeant.
She was sent to the insane ward in the
Receiving Hospital, where she had to be
strapped down on her cot. During the
afternoon she became quieter and talked
rationally. She was "divorced from her
husband eighteen months ago. and has
since been working to support herself
and girl. The constant fear that her
husband would take the girl from her
drove her insane, but it did not develop
into an acute stage until yesterday.
The Sheriff took charge of the little
girl, and sent a deputy with her to Oak
land to a Mrs. Pearson, on Tenth street,
where Mrs. Dockhan boarded.
FOR WRONGING A GIRL.
W. F. Rau. a Waiter, Arrested on
Complaint of Indignant Parents.
W. F. Rau, a waiter, was arrested la=t
night by Officers Coleman and Kane and
charged with an assault on Lena Shenk,
a girl living on Tehama street. Had he
not been taken into custody he might
have been killed, as the father of the
girl was searching for the prisoner with
evil Intent.
Rau was a friend of the girl's family
and has frequently taken her to theaters.
Her parents Intercepted one of his let
ters and discovered his love for their
daughter. They closely questioned the
firl and the information they gained In
uced them to ask Chief Lees for a war
rant for Rau'B arrest. Their request, be
ing on little evidence, was denied and
they then appealed to the Society for the
Suppression of Vice with the result that
Rau was arrested.
He denies any wrong-doing.

some Building.

with the handsome and costly Oelrlch
belts and gold medals valued- at $35 each.

Parishioners Will Give It at Mirabel
Park on May 18.

John W. McCarthy, were appointed a

train to leave at 8:45 a. m. on the day of
the picnic. Each member of the general
committee was appointed a committee on
prizes, and Lieutenant George Sullivan
was elected secretary and will receive all
prizes. Rev. Father Cullen was unani

JURY DISAGREED.
Unable to Fix Responsibility for j
Hulda Johnson's Death.
The jury in the case of William John
son, who seeks to recover $10,000 damages
from the Market-street Railway Company
. for the death of his 2-year-old daughter,
Hulda, who was killed by a Mission
street car, reported to Judge Dalngerfleld
at 10 o'clock last night that they were un
able to agree upon a verdict, and they
were ordered discharged. The jury was
evenly divided in opinion, six being in
favor of awarding damages while six
thought .the company should not be held
responsible. - . ...
The suit has been on trial for several
days, and was given to the jury late yes
terday afternoon. It was admitted that
the car was not provided with a fender,
but there was no question as to the posi
tion of the child just before the accident.
Witnesses for the prosecution testified
that she was between the rails when the
car was 100 feet away, while those for the
company maintained that she was cross
ing the street and ran in front of the car.
The motorman. admitted that he saw the
child but did not reverse the current for
fear it might disable the car.
WILL CAMP AT SANTA CRUZ.
League of the Cross Cadets Select the
Place for Their Annual Outing.
The annual encampment (if the League
of the Cross Cadets will be held this year
at Santa Cruz. Colonel William P. Sul
livan visited the city by the sea on Sun
day, accompanied by Captain "Jack"
Duff v. -- They inspected the 'various sites
available and finally selected one,-. that
fills all requirements.
: It Is within a stone's throw of the beach
and is sheltered from any wind that may
come In from the ocean. It is on the
fashionable promenade and is lighted with
electricity. The boys will leave here on
July 16 and will be in camp eight days.
It Is expected that fully 400 boys will
spend their vacation at the camp. Last
year they went to Santa Rosa, where
they were royally entertained.
SUNDAY NIGHT THIEVES.
Davis-Street Cigar Store Bobbed of
Valuable Goods.
Thieves entered' the store of Henry
Lichtenstein, 25 Davis street, Sunday
night and carried off 2700 cigars valued at
$60 a thousand. The Yale lock was picked
and carried off with the other booty.
This is the second time within five
months that this store has been burglar
ized and Lichtenstein is now wondering
what good it does him to have police pro
tection and pay extra for a special watch
man. On both occasions the work was
evidently that of novices at the business
of burglary, but in the former no clew
was ever found to the perpetrators. Mr.
Lichtenstein says that owing to the ne
cessity for additional police protection at
the ferries on Sunday nights the district
in which his store lies is left without ad
equate protection and thieves fined easy
work.
Mechanics' Fair Plans.
At a meeting last night of the directors
of Mechanics' Institute to consider mat
ters connected with the fair to be
opened September 2 at the Pavilion, Sec
retary Cumming reported that a number
of people had already applied for space.
It was decided to send. out next week the
preliminary announcement of the fair,
immediately after which the work of gel
ting the project into shape will be com
menced. A meeting will be held next Fri
day to appoint an agent to look after the
collection and installation of the exhibits.
An exhibit of the products of the Phil
ippines, the first of its kind in this coun
try, will be one of the features of tin
fair. It is being collected by Lieutenant
A. P. Hayne
The Association Lunch To-Day.
The Baptist and Christian churches will
hold forth and provide the luncheon at
the old building of the Young Men's
Christian Association, 232 Sutter street,
to-day from 11 to 2 o'clock. The ladles of
the various denominations arc vying
with each other to see who will provide
the most appetizing lunch. Yesterday the
Presbyterian ladles had a large crowd
and did a thriving business. The Metho
dists come on to-morrow and the closing
day, Friday, the Congregationallsts.
Complained to the Police.
S. Monks, who .conducts a hair store at
122 McAllister street, visited police head
quarters last night and requested the
services of a police officer to protect him
from a crowd of malicious boys who, he
said, were throwing rocks -into his store.
Monks stated to Lieutenant Birdsall that
his peace had been sadly â  disturbed for
some time past by a gang of hoodlums
who congregate in that vicinity. Police
man Pyle was detailed to put a stop to
the hair dealer's trouble. :St"\-'
Got the Full Penalty.
Patrick Bannon, one of the notorious
Bannon family, who raised a row in
Judge Mogan's court Monday, appeared
for sentence yesterday on the charge of
disturbing the peace preferred by his
mother and the charges of disturbing the
peace and vulgar language preferred by
the Judge. He was sentenced to eighteen
months in the County Jail, being six
months on each of the three charges.
A New Passenger Agent.
H. M. McGregor, the popular traveling
passenger agent of the Canadian Pacific,
has resigned his position and will leave
shortly for Alaska to take up Interests Of
his own. He has been succeeded by F.
V. "Valentine, formerly in the employ of
the company at Tacoma. Mr. Valentine
will arrive here within "a day or so and
will make this end of the line his head
quarters.
Directors Did Not Meet.
By request of a large number of the
stockholders the directors of the Central
Pacific did not hold their regular annual
meeting this morning. There are a number
of documents in preparation in New York
City which it is desired that the directors
consider and In order that there may be
ample time to finish them postponement
was ordered to April 28.
Used Knockout Drops.
Ernest Fuhrman and John Peters, ex
convicts and users of "knock-out drops,"
were convicted by Judge Conlan yester
day on the charge of vagrancy, and sen
tenced to five months each in the County
Jail. When arrested Friday night they
had a stranger from New Zealand in
tow.
Dr. Parkhurst, the reformer, de
clares the big cities of America are
preparing for a great revolution, and
he tells all about it in next Sunday's
Call.
A Waiter's Death.
The man who was taken from _53*_
Third street to the City Receiving Hos
pital last Monday night and who died
there, was Identified at the Morgue as
William Van .Ness, a waiter, well known
around the race tracks. Death was due
j to natural causes.

Absolute Cure for Dyspepsia

born Things.

GUALALA HAD
HER CUSTOMARY
TURN OF SPEED
Led Aluminum Out a
Scant Head.
WYOMING, PREMIER ACROBAT
LED HIS COMPANY OUT IN REG
ULAR RAG-TIME.
Horton Had. His Colors Lowered by
Monta_n_s Alary's Garter a
â Three Choices â¢
Downed.
Gualala, the fast filly which races in
the colors of Marcus Daly, tho Montana
millionaire, and Is trained by the veteran
Dan Dennlson, celebrated her sixth vic
tory at Oakland track yesterday. She
â¢was allotted XX) pounds In the six-furlong
handicap, and with McNichols on her
back tiptoed her company all the way,
boating out Aluminum a scant head In
1:14 flat. The latter, thrown in with a
"catch," came very near being a surprise.
Allyar, which seems to have no speed
since Pat Dunne let him go, run a disap
pointing third.
There was nothing connected with the
day's racing deserving of special mention
outside of an acrobatic performance or
two. Three favorites were disposed of.
The "corkscrew twister" turned by
Wvomlng. with Shields tip. in the mile
and a sixteenth run, was appreciated by
all those speculators who backed the
horse on Saturday* last, when he ruled
favorite and ran unplaced. Yesterday,
over the same route, ti to 1 was chalked
against the Brown entry and he won with
ludicrous ease. De Happy, a 12 to 1 shot,
finished second, and -Roadrunner, against
which 100 to 1 was laid, ran third. Hardly,
the favorite, showed to poor advantage.
Lady Meddlesome, favorite for the in
troductory sprint over the Futurity
course, was unable to get a mark. She
showed speed to the stretch and then
fell back beaten. Thorpe managed to get
the second choice Dolore away in front
and was never headed. Heigh Ho, with
116 pounds up, finished strong, causing the
â winner to be ridden out.
The mile and a sixteenth selling run
saw a brilliant array of "crabs" face the
barrier. Einstein, with Bassinger in the
paddle, closed a slight favorite over
Major Hooker, and leading most of the
"journey won cleverly from the latter.
Kllev landed Kosemaid In the show.
"Daggle" Smith's cam.- colt Horton was
beaten again in the third number, a
sprinting affair. Slow to begin, he made
up a deal of ground, but could not catch
Montanus, which won easily. Ach, a 100
to 1 shot, led into the stretch and then

Track Notes.
"Billy" Randall's shifty performer,
Tammany Hall 11, which Injured himself
so badly at Ingleside some weeks ago by
running into a fence,. was destroyed yes
terday, as blood poisoning had made its
appearance. The animal was owned by
William Tuohy of Anaconda, Mont.
It is said that W. H. Martin, suspended
by the Oakland officials for several pe
culiar looking rides, will shortly be rein
stated.
Charley Quinn seems to possess the oc
cult power of mind reading. When things
had quieted down ln the betting ring and
everybody else was aboard, Quinn backed
Wyoming in the books that would take
his money. . " . -
It was reported around the track that
Powell had been suspended for his ride
on Ping, but the rumor proved an Idle
one. Ivy is having a right jolly time of
lt. and it would be cruelty to set him
down. Powell can ride a despised out.
sider, but when It comes' to tossing the
leg over a short priced one, like Nash
Turner, he Is seized with an attack of
wind colic and curvature of the spine.
Cash Sloan was so pleased with Henry
Shields' bareback ride on Wyoming that
he bid the horse up $250 over his entered
selling price of $400.

THE SAN EBAXCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 12, 1899.
SHOT THE MAN WHO
WRECKED HIS HOME
JOSEPH CLARK ON TRIAL FOR
' MURDER.
Elements of Self-Defense and Tem
porary Insanity Will Enter
Into the Defense of
the Prisoner.
The trial of Joseph Clark for the killing
of Joseph P. Gross, which occurred No
vember 30, 1898, in front of the fire engine
house on Stockton street, between Pa
cific and Broadway, Is progressing
rapidly in Judge Dunne's 'court. The
trial promises interesting developments.
Clark, the defendant, claims that Gross
had been intimate with his wife, and, in
fact, Mrs. Clark left her husband about
three months before the shooting, went
to live away from him and took. Gross
Into her home as a boarder. Clark says
that the dead man broke up his home
through questionable means and the loss
of his wife turned his mind arid culmi
nated in his arrest for murder. Although
the defendant does not deny that he de
liberately killed Gross, a certain element
of self-defense will enter into the case, as
it Is contended that when the two men
met just before the killing Gross made
a threatening movement and then a bul
let from Clark's revolver ended his life.
Louis P. Boardman, who made the last
appeal for Durrant before the United
States Supreme Court, and H. Dlgby
Johnston are representing the prisoner.
Reliance for acquittal of the defendant
rests upon the contended justification of
the killing through Gross' acts in wreck
ing Clark's home, while if this fails a
technical fight on the ground of Insanity,
the element of self-defense and other
points will be made*
After the jury -was impaneled yesterday
George W. Russell was placed upon the
stand to explain the chart he had drawn
of the engine-house, where the killing
took place, Thomas J. Coogan, a fireman,
was next sworn. He proved a reluctant
witness, at least when it came to cross
examination, and caused heated â words
between the court and counsel for the
defense. He admitted having been to
Mrs. Clark's home with the dead man
and had been told by the deceased that
he "boarded" with the defendant's wife.
Dr. Campbell Ford was called to testify
that he was summoned after the fatal
shot had been fired and found Gross dead,
and Fireman John J. Mahoney was put
on the stand to testify to equally formal
matters, after which the case went over
till to-day.
Death of Capitalist Gregory.
George D. Gregory, a retired capitalist,
70 years old, died at St. Luke's Hospital
last Sunday from an abscess caused by
colliding with a post in the dark a short
time ago. -v,.':>

C. D. BUNKER. Secretary.
NOTICE is hereby given by order of the Board
of Directors of the OCEANIC STEAMSHIP
COMPANY that a meeting of the stockholders
of said Company has been called by said
Board, to be held on FRIDAY, the second day
of June, A. D. 1899, at eleven o'clock in the
forenoon of said day, at the principal place of
business of -aid Company, at the building
where the said Board of Directors usually
. meets, namely, at the office of said Company,
number 327 Market et.. in the City and County
of San Francisco, State of California; that
the object of said meeting is to consider and
act upon the proposition that said Company
create a bonded indebtedness of two million
five hundred thousand dollars ($2,500,000 00), in
United States gold coin, for the purpose of
raising money to complete the construction
of Its steamships and their equipment, for
use in the business of this corporation, and
to purchase and pay for any other property
within the purposes of this Company; and to
secure the bonded Indebtedness so proposed
to be created by a mortgage upon it* steam
and sailing ships and all other property of
said Company now owned or hereafter to be
acquired by said Company.
By. order of the Board of Directors of the
Oceanic Steamship Company. . *
[Corporate Seal.] E. H. SHELDON.
Secretary of the Oceanic Steamship Com-
pany.
MEET to-day at UNCLE HARRIS', 15 Grant
aye.. to borrow money on any old thing.

PROPOSALS.
PROPOSALS for fresh beef and muttonâ Of-
fice Chief Commissary, San Francisco, Cal.,
April 11, 1899. Sealed proposals for furnishing
and delivering fresh beef and mutton will be
received here and at offices of commissaries
at Alcatraz Island, Angel Island. Benicia i
Barracks. Fort Mason, Presidio of San Fran-
cisco and San Diego Barracks, Cal., until
11 o'clock a. m., May 11, 1899, and then
opened. Information furnished on applica-
tion. Envelopes containing proposals should
be indorsed "Proposals for Fresh Beef and
Mutton," and addressed to undersigned or
to commissary at post to be supplied. S. F.
THORN, Major, Chief Commissary.
SAN FRANCISCO. Cal., Inarch 15, Sealed
proposals, in triplicate, will be received here
until 12 o'clock, m.. April 14, 1899, and then
opened, for new boiler, repairs to engine and
docking, cleaning and repairing U. &. steam-
er General McDowell. Government reserves
right to reject or accept any or all bids in
whole or In part. Preference given to articles
of domestic production. Conditions of pride
and quality (Including in the price of foreign
productions the duty thereon) being equal,
and such preference given to articles of Amer-
ican production produced on the Pacific Coast
to extent of consumption required by the pub-

fixtures, finishes, etc., for the Hall of Justice
ana Morgue buildings.
Office of the Clerk of the Board of super-
visors of the City and County of San Fran-
cisco, April 4. 1899.
In accordance with resolution No. 2587
(Fourth Series) of the Board of Supervisors,
sealed proposals will be received in open ses-
sion of the Board of Supervisors on MON-
DAY AFTERNOON, April 17. 1899. from 2:30
to 3 o'clock, for furnishing all the furniture,
fixtures, finish*., etc., for the Hall of Justice
and Morgue buildings, ln strict accordance
with the plans and specifications prepared by
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, and filed in
the office of the clerk of the Board of Super-
visors on April 3. IS9V, copies of which cari
be seen by intending bidders at office of
Messrs. Shea & Shea, architects, at No it
Montgomery street.
Bidders will bid on this work with the un-
derstanding that all the material mentioned
in the specifications shall be manufactured In
the State of California, and that all labor
necessary in the manufacture of said mate-
rial and all labor necessary in the erection
and completion of the work mentioned In the
specifications shall be performed by parties
residing ln the State of California.
And it Is to be distinctly understood that
eight hours shall constitute a day's work for
persons employed on the above work, and
that the minimum rate of wages to be paid
shall be two ($2) dollars per day.
Bidders will also be required to submit with
their bids a certified check on some bank ln
the city and county of San Francisco In a
sum equal to one-tenth (1-10) of the amount
of their bid. and the party or parties to
whom the contract or contracts may be
awarded will be required to enter into a bond
ln a sum equal to one-fourth (%) 0 f the
amount of their contract, signed by two or
more good and sufficient sureties, to be ap-
proved by the Board of Supervisors, for the
faithful performance of tne contract.
Bidders will estimate and state a price on
the following subdivisions of the material to
be supplied and work performed, to wit*

HUNTINGTON TRIUMPHS
BY A MASTER STROKE
Secret of the Deal in Which Crocker
Capitulated.
The Aged Manipulator Tricks His Young Rival Into
Submission and Obtains a Power for Which Be
Fought in Vain for Years.
THE truth in reference to the re
cent railroad deal in which H. E.
Huntington was shorn of his au
thority as the first assistant to
his. uncle, C. P. Huntington, is
gradually becoming public property.
Railroad people, local financiers and
men of affairs see clearly that C. F.
Huntington, crafty and clever, has won
perhaps the most emphatic diplomatic
triumph in his lons and brilliant career
as a financier. Instead of jeopardizing
or sacrificing to the Crocker and Stan
ford factions in the Southern Pacific
Company any of his vast interests or
his coveted power Huntington has
tricked his bitter enemies in the com
pany Into a submission to his absolute
will. Through the inexperience of
George Crocker the wily president of
the Southern Pacific has gained in a
single day what had been for years an
impossibility. Crocker has abandoned
to the old man everything and has re
ceived nothing in return. Huntington
has given to Crocker the pomp of bogus
power and retained for himself the
substance of authority. More than
that the old railroad magnate has
wheedled into his hands that -which no
struggle would, nave gained for him.
He has destroyed the danger of the
Crocker and Stanford factions that
constantly menaced him and after
years of intense antagonisms and
shrewd manipulation he is now the
dominating spirit in the great corpora
tion of which before he was simply a
factor. He has given to George
Crocker the mantle of the second vice
president and has sneaked oft* with the
throne.
In this aspect the recent deal in the
railroad company is of exceptional pub-
lie interest. For th* 1 first time in the
history of the families who projected
and built the Central Pacific and
Southern Pacific railroads Huntington
has complete control and he has re
ceived it from the son of his bitter
enemy. During his lifetime Charles F.
Crocker fought the ambition of Hunt
ington. At his death Fred Crocker con
tinued the battle. ..eland Stanford had
also bitterly opposed the pretensions of
Huntington and Mrs. Stanford has fol
lowed in her husband's footsteps. The
Stanford and Crocker factions were al
ways allies. George Crocker went into
the Southern Pacific Company to con
tinue the battle which his father and
brother had fought and to hold over
the head of Huntington the firebrand
Of opposition.
In this struggle, continuing through
years, Huntington was always more
powerful than his enemies, but in order
to win much he had to give some and
now, without giving anything, he has
won all. Shrewd men. who under
stand the situation, shrug their should
ers when they refer to George Crocker,
and declare knowingly and with a
smile, that wiser men in the field of
finance and diplomacy have discovered
to their cost that C. P. Huntington Is
more than their match in that game
where something is obtained for noth
ing. In other words, railroad men
have awakened to the interesting
knowledge that C. P. Huntington has
sold George Crocker a gold brick and
did not even have the courtesy to allow
Crocker to test the filings.
This is why railroad men consider the

recent deal the greatest triumph in
Huntington's career of craft. He can
do now as he pleases with the vast in
terests involved in the Southern Pa
cific Company. There is no longer a
check upon his judgment or upon his
policy, and those who should know
say that Mrs, Stanford, to say the
least, is fearful for the future of her in
terests. No one believes seriously for
a moment that C. P. Huntington has
sacrificed or in any way injured his
nephew, H. E. Huntington, who has
been for years the trusted agent and
personal representative of his uncle on
this coast.
When Colonel Fred Crocker was bit
terly engaged in the war upon Hunt
ington and had the Stanford interests
in complete alliance with him, Hunt
ington found it absolutely necessary
for the protection of his interests on this
side of the continent to send his nephew
to this city. The younger Huntington
watched these interests carefully, criti
cally and with satisfaction to his uncle.
The younger man enjoyed the utmost
confidence of the elder, and when
George Crocker, upon the death of his
brother, entered the directory of the
Southern Pacific Company, avowedly
to oppose the policy of the president,
young Huntington had occasion still
to retain the deepest friendship of his
uncle.
Nor has he lost it now, for C. P.
Huntington is too seasoned a manipu
lator to leave his stronghold unguarded
even after the capitulation of the
enemy. Notwithstanding the fact that
George Crocker has surrendered the
Stanford interests as well as his own
C. P. Huntington has still the need of
the nephew. "While the position of first
assistant to the president has been
abolished George Crocker has received
nothing in return. He has none of the
power which the first assistant wielded.
Crocker has not been advanced in au
thority. He has simply made C. P.
Huntington the dictator of the great
corporation. As long as George Crocker
is obedient to the will and subservient
to the policy of C. P. Huntington there
will be no need of a first assistant to
the president. But when George Crocker
again swings that firebrand of his the
nephew will once more appear. He will
not be far away, as he is still a director
of the Southern Pacific Company and
the president of the Market-street
Company. C. P. Huntington has trap
ped too many old birds to be caught,
himself by a young one.
Ever since the death of Colonel Fred
Crocker this great denouement in the
railroad has been expected and feared.
Colo-Pel Fred Crocker had fought with
uncompromising effort against the per
sistent encroachments of Huntington in
the company. In this struggle Fred
Crocker was the representative" of the
Stanford interests as well as his own.
He could not be browbeaten, and Hunt
ington, shrewd as he is, could not trick
him. HUS
When Colonel Fred Crocker died the
Stanford and Crocker interests were
without a logical representative or a
champion to continue* the campaign
against Huntington. Will Crocker could
not be prevailed upon to take up the
burden for a double reason. He knew
nothing about the intricate art of rail
roading of which Huntington is a mas
ter, and besides his preference was for
Other pursuits. As a matter of fact
George Crocker knew no more than his
brother 'about railroads. George Crocker
had given absolutely no attention either
to railroads or to finance, in both of
which Huntington rules without a
rival.
In desperation, however, George
Crocker was chosen as the representa
tive of the two factions which had
fought so lone to preserve the integrity
of their interests against the under
mining influence of the President.
Crocker had full knowledge of his in
experience. Utterly without training
he was selected to give battle to one of
the shrewdest and craftiest manipula
tors of the age and admittedly one of
the greatest financiers of the century.
Through long years of uncertainty,
with war within and war without,
Huntington had handled with a superb
mastery the enormous interests that he
had acquired. When other railroads
had come upon evil times and receivers
were at their doors Huntington smil
ingly continued to mind his own busi
ness, interfered discreetly in the busi
ness of other people and paid his inter
est regularly. He had locked horns
with the Goulds and the Vanderbilts
and had not come second best from the
contest. He had been in many a great
battle of manipulation and intrigue of
finance and as some one said: "No one
was ever able to get his knife under
the pie crust of C. P. Huntington."
And then George Crocker went and
purchased a pie knife. It was his an
nounced purpose to oppose Huntington
in everything. The old man could not
force him out altogether and George
Crocker was determined not to be
fooled. If he was no match in diplo
macy for Huntington he could at least
fight. The battle has ended. Th*
Crocker and Stanford interests in the
Southern Pacific Company are now
what they never were before â subser
vient to C. P. Huntington. What
Charles F. â¢ Crocker, Leland Stanford,
Colonel Fred Crocker and Mrs. Stan
ford fought for has been lost and
George Crocker thinks he has won a
victory. C. P. Huntington never says
what he thinks. â¢Â«
In this factional fight for supremacy
in the Southern Pacific Company a
feature of minor interest has been dis
played. During the lifetime of Colonel
Fred Crocker the big yellow building
of the corporation was divided into two
bitterly antagonistic factions. The fight
between Colonel Crocker and Hunting
ton was taken up by their respective
followers. Heads of departments were
in one camp or the other. They chose
their standard as they believed success
would come. There were Huntington
men and Crocker men. and the feeling
was so bitter that even clerks were ar
rayed against each other.
This condition of affairs has con
tinued to the present time. Railroad
men are wondering now if the follow
ers of Crocker and those in the Stan
ford faction will feel the weight of
Huntington's anger. Crocker's capitu
lation has made the old man absolute
master of the great corporation after
years of struggle, worry, intrigue and
culminating triumph. It may be that
Huntington will now pay his debts of
spite.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, APRIL 12, 1899.
HIS TIRELESS WORK
FINDS ITS REWARD
General Dickinson
the Recipient of a
.Handsome Token
From Jiis Jldmip
er_,
YESTERDAY General John H. Dickinson was the recipient of a handsome
silver punch bowl, presented to him by the attaches of the investigating
committee of State institutions appointed by the Senate two years ago, as
a manifestation of their appreciation of his efforts while chairman
of the committee. Among the presentees were: Frank M. Brandon, sec
retary of the committee; Bliss & Flaville and Reid - Brothers, architects;
Messrs. Mott and Van Court, stenographers, and the other clerks and secre
taries of the investigating body. â  .-,' 7
When the 'committee received its commission two years ago it immediately,
set to the task of making, a complete and thorough examination into the af
fairs of many State institutions. This required several months of- most assid
uous labor, during which time Dickinson performed his share in such a man
ner as to relieve the burdens of his associates. As a result a voluminous and
graphic report was handed over to the last session of the legislative body.
The general's energy proved untiring, and his assistants recognizing this,
took occasion to present him with a memento of their respect and confi
dence. w3_&
The bowl is a handsome speciment of workmanship, having inscribed on
one side the following: "To General John H. Dickinson, from Appreciative
Friends. March 19, 1599." On the other side are the initials, ''J. H. D."
VANDERBILTS
PAID LITTLE
FOR THEIR ROAD
'They Got a Majority
of All the Stock.
WORK WILL BEGIN AT ONCE
FREEMAN & JAMES TO FLOAT
TWO MILLION DOLLARS.
The Entire Issue of the West Shore
Road Taken by Them at
Eighty-Seven Cents on.
the Dollar.
Yesterday the terms of the contract by
which Freeman & James of New York
City, agents for the Vanderbilts, came
into control of the West Shore Railroad
were given out to the public, and the an
nouncement was made that the work of
construction would be begun within the
next sixty days.
By the terms of the contract Freeman
& James take at the rate of nt cents on
the dollar all of the $2,000,000 bond issue
which the Joost people have for so long
tried unsuccessfully to float. Also they
take 67 per cent of the stock of the com
pany, leaving the balance .to.be. divided
among the stockholders in amounts pro
portionate to their present holdings.
The New York agent's further guarantee
to begin work on the line immediately
and that the construction shall be of the
best. There will be a large amount of
bridge work required along the route and
these bridges, it Is specified, shall be
bullded of steel. :.".*;.â .;. ',
It was denied yesterday, and with evi
dent good reason, that Joose had ever ac
quired a franchise in this county, al
though he had secured his rights of way.
This was one of the points that stood in
the way of the consummation of the deal
and was thoroughly investigated' by the
local attorneys of the New York agents.
As a result, they reported that the fran
chise matter was in as good shape as
every other phase of the transaction .and
the deal was ordered closed.
Work on the other lines is again going
on. Ninety miles of new track has just
been laid on the Utah and Pacific from
Milford, Utah, westward, and Colonel
Jones has lately returned from the East
with another block of money to go ahead
with the Fresno-Monterey connection.
The Utah line Is now but 320 miles away
from its nearest railway connection in
this State, and it is said that no more
time will be dallied away In its construc
tion now that the western terminal point
has been established.
BURGLARS WORK UNDER
THE POLICE'S EYES
RANSACKED A SALOON AND
ARE UNMOLESTED.
A burglary of unusual daring was re
ported to the police last night, and as it
occurred immediately opposite the Central
Station Captain Wittman and his officers
feel chagrined, that they did not capture
i hi- bold offenders.
When Henry Clasby, proprietor of the
Tulare Exchange, at 231 Larkin street, ar
rived at his place of business- yesterday
morning he was astounded to find his sa
loon in a wrecked condition. According
to his statement it looked as if a cyclone
had struck it. Empty bottles. were scat
tered about the place, as were also orange
skins and empty boxes. Pools of liquid
were all over the floor, and behind the bar
were empty spaces where bottles of wine
and whisky once reposed.
A further examination of the premises
disclosed the fact that the card machine
had been wrecked and its nicker recepta
cle emptied. The nickel-in-the-slot ma
chine attached to the telephone was torn
from Us fastening by the burglars. A note
pasted on the safe informed its owner that
he was "in a barrel, but his safe was un- I
touched." The thieves attempted to open
it, but were unsuccessful, much to Mr.
Clasby's satisfaction, as a large amount of
money had been placed there the night be
fore.
In searching for a clew to the unwel
come visitors the officers last night dis
covered that they had burst in a door
leading to the alley way on the Ash
avenue side, and then, without danger
from molestation, they cautiously removed
the transom over the back door leading to
the saloon. The burglars then crawled
through and began their depredation. Once
inside, they filled themselves with liquor
and then loaded themselves down with
their spoils and departed.
Their work was so stealthily done that
the people living upstairs were undis
turbed, nor waa any passer-by attracted
by the unusual visit.
Detectives have been detailed on the
case but have not as yet been able to lo
cate the thieves. â¢

black and natural oak moldings for pic
ture frames in the very latest patterns
at Sanborn & Vail's, 741 Market street. *
TO BE MARRIED BENEATH
A CANOPY OF BLOSSOMS
Dr. T. E. Shumate and Miss Freda Ortmann,
Who Are to. Be United This Evening".
BENEATH a canopy of fragrant blossoms and surrounded by their most
intimate friends, Dr. T. K. Shumate and Miss Freda Ortmann will be
united in marriage by Father Martin of Benicia this evening at the
home of the bride's parents, 1901 Scott street. ! â 
â¢ The popularity of the groom, who is one of the best known of the young
er physicians of this city, and the charming personality of the bride* adds
interest to the ceremony. Miss M. Enright of this city and Miss A. I.aflin
of San Diego will act as bridesmaids, and Dr. J. Smith and P. Moore are to
be . groomsmen.
Nearly a hundred friends will witness the ceremony and participate in
the merrymaking afterward. A large building in the rear of the mansion will
be devoted to dancing, and a covered archway, lighted with incandescent
lamps, will be stretched from the house to the dance hall.
A few days after their marriage the happy couple will depart for Eu
rope and will be gone four months. On their return they will take up res
idence in a beautiful home on California street, which was presented by the
bride's father, John F. Ortmann, the well-known capitalist..
Dr. Shumate has been practicing medicine in this city for the past five
years.- and during that short period has established a name for himself.
He has been associated with Dr. Keoney, physician to the Police Depart
ment, and has practically acted as Dr. Keeney's assistant, attending to the
minions of the law during their Illness. He graduated from Cooper Medical
College in the class of '94. Previous to that he graduated from the depart
ment of pharmacy of the University of California. V"
Dr. Shumate has a happy faculty of making friends, and the tatter have
shown their regard for him by sending himself and bride many costly gifts.
GRAVE SCANDAL
IN COLLECTOR
LYNCH'S OFFICE
Illegal Fees Extorted
From Chinese.
BOAST OF SEARCHER WAIT
;â¢ .
DUPLICATE CERTIFICATES' OB
TAINED THROUGH HIM.
Lawyer Schaertzer Refused to Let
His Client Be Bled and the
Grafting Scheme Was
Exposed.
A scandal has developed in the office of
Internal Revenue Collector Lynch. The
suicide of Cashier Norton under the Wel
burn administration and the exposure and
flight of his principal have barely grown
cold when an incipient case of wrong do
ing comes to light. It is a flagrant viola
tion of the Federal statutes, and if not ex
posed at this time and checked would
no doubt, prove to be the entering wedge
for greater abuses.
. It has come to' the knowledge of The
Call that under a flimsy pretense a cer
tain class of persons doing business with
the office are taxed $10 each for a service
which the law declares shall be performed
without any charge whatsoever. The fact
that Chinese persons are the only suffer
ers and that brokers for Chinese and a
hanger-on of the revenue office are the
beneficiaries is no palliation for the of
fense. Somebody has been grafting a bud
of profit on the Federal tree in defiance of
the laws and in the "oppression of a class
of people too ignorant to know how to
resist' the extortion.
The 'matter came to the knowledge of j
The Call yesterday through the refusal of
H. C. Schaertzer, a lawyer, to permit his
client to be bled out of $10 which he was
not required by law to pay. Mr. Schaertz
er. had a Chinese client, a laborer, who had
lost his registration certificate and who
was anxious to get a duplicate from In
ternal Revenue Collector Lynch. Under
the statute the Collector must furnish to
all Chinese applicants a duplicate of their
certificate of registration upon their cer
tifying that the original has been lost. For
this service the Collector is not allowed to
make any charge. This particular laborer
wished to leave for a cannery in Alaska
in a day or two, and he employed Mr.
Schaertzer to get him, the duplicate re
quired.
The Collector was temporarily absent
from his office when Mr. Schaertzer called
with his client and an Interpreter, and he
stated his errand to Chief Deputy Frick.
Mr. Frick informed the lawyer that it
would be probably a week or more before
he would be able to supply him with a
duplicate, one reason being that several
bundles of the certificates were being
bound in a downtown printing establish
ment and that they would not be available
until the work of binding was completed.
Mr. Schaertzer offered to go to the print
ing office and make the search himself,
but Deputy Frick informed him that the
binder would not allow him to handle the.
certificates. â¢* In vain the lawyer pleaded
that his client had to leave the State
within a day or two and could not go with
safety without the duplicate certificate.
The deputy could do nothing for him.
The lawyer and his client had barely got
outside Mr. Lynch's office when they were
approached by an elderly man named
Wait, whom Schaertzer recognized as a
hanger-on of the revenue office and who
had been employed for three or four
months last year in making an index of
the Chinese certificates. Wait tapped the
lawyer on the shoulder and said that he
could get the duplicate for his client with
out delay if the lawyer would pay him $10

He then exhibited a paper signed by sev
eral brokers for Chinese in the following
language, as near as Mr. Schaertzer was
able to remember:
We, the undersigned, hereby agree that the
following prices shall prevail in the matter of
obtaining duplicate registration certificates for
Chinese: For preparing papers, $10; for search
ing for papers where the number is given. $10;
total, $20. For preparing papers where the
number is not given. $10; for searching for pa
pers where the number is. not given, 51"; total,
$25. In case of extra search or in cases where
extra time is required to ascertain the number
a proportionate amount will be charged for
services.
The lawyer decided that he was up
against a skin game. He turned to Wait
and flatly refused to be bled even to the.
extent of a nickel. He then 'went to his
office, whither he was followed by the
persistent Wait.
"How much are you getting for this?"
he asked the lawyer. "That is none of
your business," replied Mr. Schaertzer.
"I am getting more than the price named
in your schedule, but I don't propose to
give you a dollar."
Then Wait, glancing at the number of
the Chinaman's certificate, said that the
number was Incorrect and that the name
had not been rightly given. Having thus
raised the proper feeling of alarm Wait
fired another shaft which he expected
would find the mark.
"Collector Lynch will not allow any
body else but me in that vault to handle
the certificates, and he is willing that I
should collect something for my
trouble."
The lawyer flatly refused to enter into
negotiations with the favorite of Col
lector Lynch, and the interview termi
nated.
An hour later Collector Lynch was
asked whether he made any charge for
issuing duplicate certificates of registra
tion or whether he had authorised any
one to issue them for a fee. Mr. Lynch
replied that there was no charge for the
duplicate, and thai he had not author
ized any one to make any charge for the
service. When told that Wait had been
acting as an agent for the issuance of
duplicates and had represented, that he
had exclusive privileges in handling the
papers, Mr. Lynch, with a show of indig
nation, said that he would bar Wait from
his office, and that he had not been
aware that he, the ex-indexer, had been
making money out of the revenue office
In the next breath Mr. Lynch said tnat
Wall was a poor man, and that he had
assisted lawyers and others in getting
out the duplicates, and he (Lynch) had
"not desired to stand in the way of the
old man making a living, but he did not
indorse Wait s making a charge for his
services. The inference was drawn from
the Collector's remarks that Wait had
been receiving a gratuity voluntarily be
stowed by the lawyers and brokers
whom Wait had assisted by his knowl
edge of the Chinese certificates and the
method of picking them out.
"l won't let him in my office any
more said Mr. Lynch. Then he pro
ceeded to tell that only a few days be
fore he himself had gone to the bindery
for a Chinaman who was in a hurry to
leave the city and had got a duplicate
for himâ to accommodate the China
man.
Deputy Frick remarked that "that man
ait" had bothered him a good deal, and
he wished that he could be kept out of
the office.
About a year' ago, before Wait and an
other man had been employed to index
the certificate., The Call learned that
some clerks In Collector Lynch'- office
had been charging or receiving fees of
$3 each for finding certificates and issu
ing duplicates. One of these clerks was
said to be Dillard, the colored messen
ger; who was a principal witness against
ex-Collector .Welbiirn. It was reported
that Dillard used to work overtime in
getting out duplicates, and that he was
paid by the brokers for his trouble, but
Dillard denied that he ever received a
cent. The Call's expose at that time
drew the attention of the Secretary of
the Treasury to the matter, with the re
sult that an appropriation was made for
the purpose of having the certificates in
dexed so that duplicates could be issued
without any loss of time. All but a few
of the certificates have been indexed, and
they have been arranged and bound ac
cording to their numbers, so that a cer
tificate can be found by its number in
not more than two minutes.
In the meantime Schaertzer's client
must postpone his trip to the salmon
fisheries of 'Alaska until the binding job
is finished and he can get his duplicate,
which will be some time next week. The
present exposure wiM have the effect of
reducing the brokers' fees for duplicates
from $20 to $10, there being no necessity
for continuing the $10 fee to Mr. W ait.
